#### Division/Office  OOD/DO3

#### Established Name  Tebentafusp‐tebn

Pharmacologic Class  Bispecific gp100 peptide‐HLA‐directed CD3 T‐cell engager

#### Code name  IMCgp100

Applicant  Immunocore, LLC 
Formulation(s)  Injection, Solution, 100 mcg in 0.5 mL, Intravenous Infusion 
Dosing Regimen  20 mcg on Day 1, 30 mcg on Day 8, 68 mcg on Day 15, and 68 
mcg once every week thereafter, via intravenous infusion

Applicant Proposed 
Indication(s)/Population(s) 
For the treatment of HLA‐A*02:01‐positive adult patients with 
unresectable or metastatic uveal melanoma 
Recommendation on 
Regulatory Action   Regular Approval

#### Recommended

For the treatment of HLA‐A*02:01‐positive adult patients with 
unresectable or metastatic uveal melanoma

### 1 Executive Summary

#### Product Introduction

Tebentafusp‐tebn (referred to as tebentafusp) is a bispecific gp100 peptide‐HLA‐directed CD3 T 
cell engager. The tumor cell receptor arm of tebentafusp binds to a gp100 peptide presented by 
human leukocyte antigen‐A*02:01 (HLA‐A*02:01) on the cell surface of uveal melanoma tumor 
cells. Tebentafusp exerts its anti‐tumor effect by redirecting T cells to attack gp100‐expressing 
tumor cells.  
 
At the time of submission of the current BLA 761228 on June 23, 2021, tebentafusp was not yet 
approved for any indication in the United States. The Applicant’s proposed indication at the 
time of BLA submission was as follows:

Tebentafusp is a bispecific gp100‐
 T cell 
 for the treatment 
of HLA‐A*02:01‐positive adult patients with unresectable or metastatic uveal melanoma 
 
The proposed dosing regimen is 20 mcg on Day 1, 30 mcg on Day 8, 68 mcg on Day 15, and 68 
mcg once every week thereafter, via intravenous infusion over 15‐20 minutes.

## Conclusions on the Substantial Evidence of Effectiveness

The review team has determined that the results of Study 202, an open‐label, randomized, 
multi‐center study in 378 adult HLA‐A*02:01‐positive patients with unresectable or metastatic 
uveal melanoma (mUM) demonstrate a statistically significant and clinically meaningful 
improvement in overall survival (OS) in patients treated with tebentafusp. This trial is ongoing 
and has enrolled patients with histologically or cytologically confirmed mUM who had not 
received prior systemic therapy in the metastatic setting or prior regional liver‐directed 
therapy.  Patients were randomized 2:1 to receive tebentafusp or investigator’s choice of 
therapy (dacarbazine, ipilimumab, or pembrolizumab) until radiographic progression (except for 
patients receiving ipilimumab), unacceptable toxicity, investigator decision, or patient 
withdrawal of consent. The primary study endpoint was overall survival (OS), and key secondary 
endpoint was investigator‐assessed progression free survival (PFS) per RECIST 1.1.  
 
The data submitted by the Applicant has provided substantial evidence for the effectiveness of 
tebentafusp administered at 20 mcg on Day 1, 30 mcg on Day 8, 68 mcg on Day 15, and 68 mcg 
once every week thereafter for the treatment of HLA‐A*02:01‐positive adult patients with 
unresectable or metastatic uveal melanoma and supports regular approval for this indication. 
This conclusion is based on results from Study 202 which demonstrates a clinically and 
statistically significant improvement in OS in patients who received tebentafusp compared to

(b) (4)

#### Benefit‐Risk Assessment (BRA)

#### Benefit‐Risk Summary and Assessment

Uveal melanoma is a rare subset that accounts for <5% of all cases of melanoma in the United States but represents the most commonly

occurring intraocular malignancy in adults (Sayan 2020, Kaliki 2017). There are no FDA approved therapies for the treatment of uveal

melanoma. Patients with uveal melanoma who have systemic disease are treated with therapies approved for cutaneous melanoma, which

have demonstrated limited efficacy in this population. In the metastatic uveal melanoma setting, the National Comprehensive Cancer Network

(NCCN) guidelines version 2.2021 recommends participation in a clinical trial as the preferred management option with consideration of

monotherapy or combination regimen consisting of liver‐directed therapies (e.g. ablative procedures, embolization, isolation of perfusion,

resection or radiation), systemic therapies (e.g. immune‐oncology agents, cytotoxic regimens or trametinib), resection or radiation treatment

for limited or symptomatic extrahepatic disease, or palliative care. Despite responses as high as 90% to treatment options for localized uveal

melanoma, as many as 50% of patients will develop metastatic disease (Nichols 2016). The prognosis in patients who develop metastatic uveal

melanoma is very poor with median overall survival less than 6 months (Rantala 2019).

The Applicant has submitted data from Study 202 to support the approval of tebentafusp for the treatment of HLA‐A*02:01‐positive adult

patients with unresectable or metastatic uveal melanoma. A favorable benefit‐risk assessment has been established for tebentafusp based on

demonstration of a clinically meaningful and statistically significant improvement in OS in patients who received tebentafusp compared to

those who received Investigator’s choice of therapy which include dacarbazine, pembrolizumab or ipilimumab (HR of 0.51 [95% CI: 0.37, 0.71],

The data submitted to support the safety review of tebentafusp is adequate to characterize toxicity in patients with uveal melanoma. The

toxicity profile of tebentafusp in patients with HLA‐A*02:01 unresectable or metastatic uveal melanoma was comparable with the toxicity

observed in patients treated with Investigator’s choice of therapy in Study 202. Although there was a slightly higher incidence of SAEs observed

in patients treated with tebetafusp, overall tebentafusp appeared to be well tolerated. Fatal adverse events and permanent treatment

discontinuation in patients on tebentafusp are rare. Adverse events of special interest (AESIs) were identified in patients treated with

tebentafusp. These AESI included cytokine release syndrome (CRS), skin reactions (which includes the subcategories of rash, pruritis, pigment

change, erythema, edema, dry skin, and other changes) and elevated hepatic enzymes. Risk minimization strategies have been instituted via

#### Dimension

 Uveal melanoma is a rare subset of melanoma and accounts for <5%

of all cases of melanoma in the United States.

 The incidence of uveal melanoma is estimated to be 4 to 5 cases per

million per year (Sayan 2020; Kaliki 2017).

 Localized disease may be treated with radiation or surgery, however

almost half of patients will develop systemic disease (Sayan 2020).

Uveal melanoma is a serious and life‐

threatening condition. Patients diagnosed with

metastatic disease have a poor prognosis with

median survival <6 months (Rantala 2019).

 Non‐metastatic disease can be treated with radiation or surgery

depending on the size of the lesion.

 Patients with advanced and metastatic uveal melanoma are

encouraged to participate in clinical trials. Other options for

management include a combination approach liver‐directed

therapies, systemic therapies, and resection or radiation for limited or

symptomatic extrahepatic disease (NCCN 2021).

 Uveal melanoma is treated with the same systemic therapies used for

the management of cutaneous melanoma; however these therapies

have shown limited benefit in patients with uveal melanoma.

There are no approved therapies specifically

for uveal melanoma and no effective therapy

options once disease has metastasized. Given

the poor prognosis, there remains an unmet

medical need.

been appropriately amended to provide

warranted.

### 2  Therapeutic Context

#### Analysis of Condition

The Applicant’s Position:

Uveal melanoma is a rare and highly malignant subset of melanoma, representing < 5% of all 
melanoma cases in the United States (US; McLaughlin, 2005). Uveal melanoma is the most 
frequent primary intraocular malignancy of the adult eye (~85%; Patel, 2011; Maio, 2013). The 
incidence varies by geography, race, and age, ranging from 5.3 to 10.9 cases per million (Singh, 
2011). Uveal melanoma arises exclusively from melanocytes of the uvea, and it is biologically, 
clinically, and genetically distinct from cutaneous melanoma (CM; Jager, 2020). BRAF and 
NRAS mutations dominate the landscape in CM, whereas mutations in guanine nucleotide 
binding protein, q polypeptide, and alpha 11 dominate in UM (Shoushtari, 2014). The mode of 
disease spread is distinct between the 2 diseases, with hematogenous spread being most common 
in UM while lymphatic predominates in CM. Moreover, UM has a highly immunosuppressive 
tumor microenvironment (Jager, 2020; Rothermel, 2016). Cutaneous melanoma has one of the 
highest tumor mutational burdens (TMB), whereas the TMB of UM is among the lowest of all 
cancers (Lee, 2019; Yarchoan, 2017). The low TMB in UM results in few neoantigens, which 
limits natural antitumor immunity (Violanti, 2019), making UM less sensitive to immune 
checkpoint inhibitors than CM.

Uveal melanoma is a life-threatening disease with no effective therapy once it metastasizes. 
Despite local therapy (radiation and surgery), up to 50% of patients with UM develop systemic 
metastases, predominantly to the liver (~90% of patients) and less commonly to the lungs and 
bones (Nathan, 2015). Once patients develop metastatic UM (mUM), the prognosis and 
outcomes are dismal, with a median survival of ≤ 12 months (Rantala, 2019; Khoja, 2019). Over 
the past 40 years, there has been no significant improvement in survival for patients with mUM. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
FDA agrees with the Applicant’s characterization and assessment of the disease.

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

23 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

#### Analysis of Current Treatment Options

The Applicant’s Position:

No products are formally approved by FDA for the treatment of metastatic uveal melanoma.

#### Current Treatment Landscape for Uveal Melanoma

In contrast to other melanomas, particularly CM, current treatment options for mUM are limited 
and deemed ineffective. Metastatic UM responds poorly to cytotoxic chemotherapy, 
radiotherapy, and immunotherapy (Buder, 2013; Pereira, 2013). No systemic or local therapies 
are approved specifically for the treatment of mUM (Carvajal, 2014; Luke, 2013; Maio, 2013). 
Treatments introduced for CM over the past decade have not significantly benefited patients with 
UM, reflecting the distinct biology, genetics, and clinical course of UM. The standard of care for 
mUM is enrollment in a clinical trial (Carvajal, 2017). When clinical trials are not available, 
therapies for advanced CM are used.

Representative multicenter clinical studies or case series of at least 20 patients with mUM who 
received available therapies are summarized in Error! Reference source not found.. Studies 
with single-agent chemotherapy arms showed a median overall survival (OS) of approximately 
10 months (Buder, 2013). Single-agent immune checkpoint inhibitors (anti-cytotoxic T-
lymphocyte associated antigen-4 [CTLA-4] or anti-programmed cell death-1 [PD-1]) exhibited 
median OS similar to that of cytotoxic therapy in mUM (approximately 6 to 10 months). These 
data are generally consistent with a global meta-analysis that included > 900 patients in Phase 2 
trials in mUM, demonstrating a median OS of approximately 9 months and 1-year OS rate of 
43% across treatment modalities and lines of therapy in the metastatic setting (Khoja, 2019). In a 
meta-analysis of > 2400 first line (1L) mUM patients, median OS was about 12 months with a 1-
year OS rate of 52% (Rantala, 2019). Although the combination of anti-CTLA-4 and anti-PD-1 
has a numerically higher objective response rate (ORR), this combination has a 1-year OS rate 
similar to checkpoint monotherapy (Pelster, 2021; Piulats, 2021). Together, these data suggest 
that a new treatment option with different mechanism of action (MoA) to those used for other 
melanomas is essential to improve outcomes in mUM, a serious and life-threatening disease.

landscape. There is no FDA approved therapy for metastatic uveal melanoma. There is no 
effective standard of care for metastatic uveal melanoma.

### 3 Regulatory Background

#### U.S. Regulatory Actions and Marketing History

The Applicant’s Position:

No prior marketing applications have been submitted to the US Food and Drug Administration 
(FDA) for tebentafusp for any indication, including metastatic uveal melanoma.  No prior 
application has been submitted to any other Health Authority globally for tebentafusp for any 
indication.  No marketing application has been rejected or deemed not approvable in the US by 
FDA or any other Health Authority globally. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
FDA agrees with the Applicant.

#### Summary of Presubmission/Submission Regulatory Activity

The Applicant’s Position:

All clinical studies of tebentafusp were conducted in accordance with Good Clinical Practice, the 
Declaration of Helsinki, and local requirements. Furthermore, all studies were conducted in 
accordance with applicable regulatory guidance, including the US Food and Drug Administration 
(FDA) and European Medicines Agency (EMA) guidance.

The Applicant sought regulatory and scientific advice from health authorities, including the FDA 
and EMA, throughout the tebentafusp clinical development program for UM. Table 2 provides a 
summary of key regulatory interactions with FDA that occurred during the tebentafusp clinical 
development program for UM that are relevant to the BLA application.

Table 2: Summary of Key FDA Regulatory Interactions During the Tebentafusp Clinical

The FDA’s Assessment: 
In addition to the interactions summarized above, FDA highlights the following: 
 January 9, 2012, FDA received initial IND submission for IMCgp100‐01. 
 February 8, 2012, FDA issued Study May Proceed letter. 
 October 14, 2015, StudyIMCgp100‐102 was submitted to FDA. 
 January 21, 2016, FDA granted Orphan Drug designation to IMCgp100 for the treatment of

uveal melanoma. 
 November 14, 2016, End of Phase 2 meeting was held.  The purpose of the meeting was to

discuss the design of the proposed pivotal Study IMCgp100‐202, entitled, “A Phase 2 Open‐
Label, Multi‐Center, Randomized, Active Comparator Trial in Patients with Advanced Uveal 
Melanoma (UM) Without Prior Treatment in the Advanced or Metastatic Setting”. 
 October 12, 2018, Type B meeting was held. The purpose of this meeting was to discuss the

Applicant’s development plan and registrational strategy for tebentafusp that included a 
proposal to submit ORR data (with a target lower 95% confidence limit of ≥ 5%) from Study 
102 and supportive data from Study 202, available at the time of the planned interim 
analysis of Study 102, to support a BLA submission for accelerated approval. FDA stated that 
it was unclear whether the targeted treatment effect that excludes a lower 95% confidence 
limit of ≥ 5% for confirmed ORR meets the threshold for reasonably likely to predict clinical 
benefit and that it was also unclear whether that target treatment effect would 
demonstrate a favorable risk:benefit analysis considering the observed toxicities of 
tebentafusp.  
 April 1, 2019, FDA granted fast track designation for IMCgp100 for first‐line treatment of

metastatic uveal melanoma. 
 March 3, 2020, a teleconference was held to discuss the clinical development plan of

IMCgp100 for the treatment of patients with uveal melanoma. 
 February 18, 2021, FDA granted Breakthrough Therapy designation to tebentafusp

(IMCgp100) for treatment of HLA‐A*02:01‐positive adult patients with unresectable or 
metastatic uveal melanoma.

### 4 Significant Issues from Other Review Disciplines Pertinent to Clinical

#### Office of Scientific Investigations (OSI)

The Division of Oncology 3 consulted OSI to discuss an audit of overall trial conduct. Four 
clinical investigators were selected for audit. These were Dr. Hassel (Site 3504), Dr. Piperno‐
Neumann (Site 6101), Dr. Rutkowski (Site 6401), and Dr. Kirkwood (Site 8705) The Applicant, 
Immunocore LLC., was also inspected as this is the first marketing application from the 
Applicant and a new molecular entity.  All inspections were conducted onsite. During the 
inspection of Site 6401 it was noted that updates to the electronic database involving efficacy 
and safety data had been made after the database lock for the submitted application which 
were reportedly to be due to limited site resources affecting their ability to complete data 
cleaning prior to the submission. There was one change in the primary end point of overall 
survival (OS) for the control arm (USUBID 6401‐
).  A request for information dated 
November 10, 2021, was sent to the Applicant to determine the extent of changes impacting 
the primary efficacy endpoint of OS and secondary efficacy endpoints of PFS and BOR across all 
sites. The Applicant reported that no changes to the OS endpoint occurred in any other patient 
besides 6401‐
. Four additional patients treated at 4 different sites (other than 6401) had a 
change impacting either PFS and/or BOR. After FDA review, it was determined that these 
updates were unlikely to change the analysis of OS or PFS or to have a significant impact on 
interpretation of the study results. The other inspectional findings verified the Applicant’s 
reported clinical data with source records at these study sites. No significant GCP compliance 
deviations were observed at these sites. Based on the inspectional findings, the OSI review 
team considers the submitted data from this study reliable for this NDA and the respective 
indication.  
 
Product Quality  
There were no significant issues.

### 5 Nonclinical Pharmacology/Toxicology

## Executive Summary

Tebentafusp (IMCgp100) is a bispecific fusion protein that consists of a soluble T cell receptor 
(TCR) fused to an anti‐cluster of differentiation 3 (anti‐CD3) single‐chain variable fragment 
(scFv). The TCR domain binds a gp100 peptide (peptide fragment derived from the melanoma 
associated antigen glycoprotein 100, position 280‐288, YLEPGPVTA):human leukocyte antigen 
(HLA)‐A*02:01 complex. Glycoprotein 100 (gp100) is a type 1 transmembrane glycoprotein that 
is enriched in melanosomes. The established pharmacologic class (EPC) is bispecific gp100 
peptide‐HLA‐directed CD3 T‐cell engager. 
 
In an in vitro assay, tebentafusp bound the gp100 peptide:HLA‐A*02:01 complex with a mean 
KD value of 24 pM. Tebentafusp bound CD3 with a mean KD value of 38 nM. Average 
bifunctional binding of tebentafusp to gp100 peptide‐HLA‐A*02:01 and soluble CD3 was 88%. 
Tebentafusp exhibited binding specificity towards the HLA‐A*02:01 subtype, with EC50 values 
ranging from ~30 to 60 pM for HLA‐A*02:01‐positive cell lines. Comparatively, tebentafusp 
bound other HLA‐A subtypes with EC50 values of ≥166 pM. In HLA‐A*02:01‐positive cutaneous 
and uveal melanoma cell lines co‐incubated with peripheral blood mononuclear cells (PBMCs), 
tebentafusp treatment led to a dose‐dependent increase in IFNγ release with EC50 values 
ranging from 0.89 to 121 pM. T cell activation by tebentafusp increased cytokines IFNγ, TNF‐α, 
IL‐6, IL‐2, and MIP‐1β. In vivo, the anti‐tumor activity of tebentafusp was evaluated in mice 
engrafted with HLA‐A*02:01/gp100‐positive Mel526 human melanoma cancer cells mixed with 
PBMCs isolated from human whole blood. In this study, administration of 5 doses of 
tebentafusp resulted in tumor growth inhibition compared to controls.  
 
There are no pharmacologically relevant animal species to evaluate tebentafusp toxicity. In 
vitro, the scFv portion of tebentafusp bound to T cells in human PBMCs but did not bind PBMCs 
from mouse or monkey. Tebentafusp was evaluated in a series of in vitro studies to 
characterize potential cross‐reactivity in 35 normal human tissues by immunohistochemistry 
(IHC) and the potential for organ‐specific toxicities in functional assays using 13 normal human 
tissues. Tebentafusp did not show off‐target/cross‐reactivity in tissues outside of melanocytes, 
and alloreactivity and platelet activation were not observed. 
 
Genotoxicity and carcinogenicity studies were not warranted.

| requesting the Applicant to conduct an assay validation study to demonstrate that the assay |
|---|
| can accurately differentiate HLA‐A*02:01 from other HLA A alleles. See Section 13. |

|---|
| (TCR) fused to an anti‐cluster of differentiation 3 (anti‐CD3) single‐chain variable fragment |
| (scFv). The TCR domain binds a gp100 peptide (peptide fragment derived from the melanoma |
| associated antigen glycoprotein 100, position 280‐288, YLEPGPVTA):human leukocyte antigen |
| (HLA)‐A*02:01 complex. Glycoprotein 100 (gp100) is a type 1 transmembrane glycoprotein that |
| is enriched in melanosomes. The established pharmacologic class (EPC) is bispecific gp100 |
| peptide‐HLA‐directed CD3 T‐cell engager. |
|  |
| In an in vitro assay, tebentafusp bound the gp100 peptide:HLA‐A*02:01 complex with a mean |
| K value of 24 pM. Tebentafusp bound CD3 with a mean K value of 38 nM. Average D D |
| bifunctional binding of tebentafusp to gp100 peptide‐HLA‐A*02:01 and soluble CD3 was 88%. |
| Tebentafusp exhibited binding specificity towards the HLA‐A*02:01 subtype, with EC values 50 |
| ranging from ~30 to 60 pM for HLA‐A*02:01‐positive cell lines. Comparatively, tebentafusp |
| bound other HLA‐A subtypes with EC values of ≥166 pM. In HLA‐A*02:01‐positive cutaneous 50 |
| and uveal melanoma cell lines co‐incubated with peripheral blood mononuclear cells (PBMCs), |
| tebentafusp treatment led to a dose‐dependent increase in IFNγ release with EC values 50 |
| ranging from 0.89 to 121 pM. T cell activation by tebentafusp increased cytokines IFNγ, TNF‐α, |
| IL‐6, IL‐2, and MIP‐1β. In vivo, the anti‐tumor activity of tebentafusp was evaluated in mice |

| There are no pharmacologically relevant animal species to evaluate tebentafusp toxicity. In |
| vitro, the scFv portion of tebentafusp bound to T cells in human PBMCs but did not bind PBMCs |
| from mouse or monkey. Tebentafusp was evaluated in a series of in vitro studies to |
| characterize potential cross‐reactivity in 35 normal human tissues by immunohistochemistry |
| (IHC) and the potential for organ‐specific toxicities in functional assays using 13 normal human |

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

30 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

Tebentafusp was not evaluated in embryofetal toxicity studies. In the reproductive toxicity 
assessment provided by the Applicant, the following points are considered: (1) gp100 is 
expressed embryonically in melanocytes found in the skin, inner ear, and eyes by 7 weeks of 
gestation; (2) MHC Class I classical proteins (including HLA‐A*02:01) and transporter‐
associated‐with‐antigen‐processing (TAP) proteins required for presentation of antigen on the 
cell surface are expressed early in embryonic development; and (3) CD3‐positive T cells begin to 
populate the periphery and are capable of being activated by around 12 weeks of gestation.  
 
Fusion proteins may cross the placenta. In an embryo‐fetal development study in mice, a 
murine surrogate of blinatumomab, which has a similar molecular weight and mechanism of 
action as tebentafusp, crossed the placental barrier and fetal exposure occurred at 
pharmacologically active concentrations.  
 
Based on its size and mechanism of action, tebentafusp may cross the placenta and cause fetal 
harm, particularly the skin, inner ear, and eye, if the developing fetus is exposed to 
tebentafusp. 
 
There are no approvability issues from a pharmacology/toxicology perspective.

## Referenced NDAs, BLAs, DMFs

The Applicant’s Position:

No prior BLAs have been submitted for tebentafusp for any indication, including metastatic 
uveal melanoma.   No DMFs are utilized by any of the Contract Manufacturing Organizations 
(CMOs) that produce the drug substance (AGC in Copenhagen, Denmark) or finished product 
(Baxter, Germany), nor any other processing provider.

#### Pharmacology

Primary pharmacology

In vitro studies

Surface plasmon resonance (SPR) was used to assess binding (Study IMC 1022). The mean KD 
value of tebentafusp for the gp100 peptide‐HLA‐A*02:01 complex was 24 pM. The mean KD 
value of tebentafusp for CD3 was 38 nM. When SPR was used to evaluate the bifunctional 
binding of tebentafusp, 88% of gp100 peptide‐HLA‐A*02:01 complex‐bound tebentafusp bound 
soluble CD3.

|---|
| Tebentafusp was not evaluated in embryofetal toxicity studies. In the reproductive toxicity |
| assessment provided by the Applicant, the following points are considered: (1) gp100 is |
| expressed embryonically in melanocytes found in the skin, inner ear, and eyes by 7 weeks of |
| gestation; (2) MHC Class I classical proteins (including HLA‐A*02:01) and transporter‐ |
| associated‐with‐antigen‐processing (TAP) proteins required for presentation of antigen on the |
| cell surface are expressed early in embryonic development; and (3) CD3‐positive T cells begin to |
| populate the periphery and are capable of being activated by around 12 weeks of gestation. |
|  |
| Fusion proteins may cross the placenta. In an embryo‐fetal development study in mice, a |
| murine surrogate of blinatumomab, which has a similar molecular weight and mechanism of |
| action as tebentafusp, crossed the placental barrier and fetal exposure occurred at |
| pharmacologically active concentrations. |
|  |
| Based on its size and mechanism of action, tebentafusp may cross the placenta and cause fetal |
| harm, particularly the skin, inner ear, and eye, if the developing fetus is exposed to |
| tebentafusp. |
|  |
| There are no approvability issues from a pharmacology/toxicology perspective. |

| In vitro studies |
|---|
| Surface plasmon resonance (SPR) was used to assess binding (Study IMC 1022). The mean K D |
| value of tebentafusp for the gp100 peptide‐HLA‐A*02:01 complex was 24 pM. The mean K D |
| value of tebentafusp for CD3 was 38 nM. When SPR was used to evaluate the bifunctional |
| binding of tebentafusp, 88% of gp100 peptide‐HLA‐A*02:01 complex‐bound tebentafusp bound |
| soluble CD3. |
|  |

Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

The Applicant also used SPR to assess the ability of tebentafusp to bind the gp100 peptide 
complexed with other HLA‐A2 subtypes (HLA‐A*02:02, 03, 05, 06, and 07; Study IMC 1040). In 
this study, tebentafusp was affinity enhanced to bind a heteroclitic (e.g., sequence‐modified) 
version of the gp100 peptide, in which the C‐terminal amino acid was substituted from an 
alanine to a valine to provide binding stability on HLA‐A*02. As shown in Table 3, the binding of 
IMCgp100 (tebentafusp) was reduced with other A2 subtypes as compared to HLA‐A*02:01, as 
measured by relative residence half‐life values.

Table 3: Relative residence half‐life for tebentafusp (IMCgp100) across HLA‐A*02 subtype

#### heavy chains

(Source: Applicant table reproduced from Study IMC 1040) 
 
The Applicant conducted cellular assays to support the HLA‐A*2 subtype binding data. Briefly, 
plasmids encoding the subtypes HLA‐A*02:01, HLA‐A*02:02, HLA‐A*02:04, HLA‐A*02:05, and 
HLA‐A*02:06 were generated under the control of a human promoter. These plasmids were 
transduced into the SK‐Mel28 HLA‐A*02‐negative cutaneous melanoma cell line and cells were 
incubated overnight with increasing concentrations of tebentafusp in the presence of PBMCs. T 
cell activation was assessed via IFNγ production ELISPOT (Study IMC 1061).

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

32 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

The Applicant assessed the ability of tebentafusp to inhibit the proliferation of tumor cells that 
are positive for HLA‐A*02:01, including the HLA‐A*02:01‐positive cutaneous melanoma (CM) 
cell line, Mel624, and the uveal melanoma (UM) cell lines, mel‐202 and 92‐1 (Study IMC 1061). 
Due to limited cell line availability for UM, the two HLA‐A*02:01‐negative cell lines (92‐1 and 
mel‐202) were transduced with a construct to express HLA‐A*02:01 and β2M under the control 
of a human promoter to allow for physiological levels of HLA‐A*02:01 expression. Tumor cells 
were incubated with non‐tumor specific PBMCs from healthy human donors and increasing 
concentrations of tebentafusp. T cell activation was determined by measuring IFNγ release 
(ELISPOT), while tumor cell killing was determined by the IncuCyte cell killing assay. Briefly, 
NucView is a DNA dye‐based reagent that is membrane permeable and does not fluoresce 
unless bound to DNA. In its native state, NucView contains a substrate that prevents binding to 
DNA. However in the presence of active caspase‐3/7, this substrate is cleaved to release the 
dye to bind DNA. Upon binding to DNA, the dye will emit fluorescence at 488 nm counts are the 
number of objects that are fluorescing per unit area. 
 
Data from the IFNγ ELISPOT assay (representative data from one PMBC donor) is shown in 
Figure 1. Incubation of tumor cell lines with PMBCs and tebentafusp led to a dose‐dependent 
increase in IFNγ release, indicating a tebentafusp‐mediated redirected PMBC response against 
the HLA‐A*02:01‐positive tumor cell lines. The EC50 values for Mel624, mel‐202 HLA‐A*201/ 
β2M, and 92‐1 HLA‐A*201/ β2M were 121, 0.89, and 53 pM, respectively.

Figure 1: PMBCs release IFNγ following incubation with tebentafusp (IMCgp100) and HLA‐

#### A*02:01 positive tumor cell lines

(Source: Applicant figure reproduced from Study IMC 1061)

Representative data from the IncuCyte killing assay is shown in Figure 2. The graphs show the 
accumulation of apoptotic target cells over a 47 hour time course. Uveal melanoma cells were

#### Figure 2: Cell kill kinetics of tebentafusp

(Source: Applicant figure reproduced from Study IMC 1061) 
 
Similar IFNγ and T cell killing data were seen when antigen‐positive cutaneous melanoma cell 
lines were incubated with tebentafusp in the presence of CD4+ and CD8+ T cells from healthy 
human donors as well as donor PBMCs from patients with stage 4 melanoma (Study IMC 1014, 
IMC 1015, IMC 1017, IMC 1020). 
 
The Applicant performed an assay to determine if T cell activation by tebentafusp induced 
release of proinflammatory cytokines (Study IMC 1020). In this study, tebentafusp was 
incubated with HLA‐A*02:01‐positive Mel526 CM cells and HLA‐A*02:01‐negative A375 
melanoma cells co‐cultured with PBMCs as effector cells. Cytokine release was measured via 
the Luminex assay. HLA‐A*02:01‐positive Mel526 cells incubated overnight with 1 nM 
tebentafusp showed release of proinflammatory cytokines, while HLA‐A*02:01‐negative A375 
cell lines did not (Figure 3).

Comparable results were seen in co‐culture assay with PMBCs isolated from patients with 
cutaneous melanoma (Study IMC 1046).  
 
Tebentafusp exhibits its pharmacologic activity through the redirected activation of CD3+ T 
cells. To test whether the activity of tebentafusp could be blocked by corticosteroids, 
tebentafusp‐mediated T cell activation assays (IFNγ ELISPOT) were performed (Study IMC 
1018). Mel526 cells were co‐incubated with CD8+ T cells and 1 nM tebentafusp, with increasing 
concentrations of hydrocortisone. As shown in Figure 4, tebentafusp‐mediated T cell activity 
(via measurement of IFNγ release) was inhibited by hydrocortisone in a dose‐dependent 
manner.

#### Figure 4: Hydrocortisone inhibition of CD8+ T cell activation by tebentafusp

(Source: Applicant figure reproduced from Study IMC 1018) 
 
Tebentafusp redirects T cell activity through its anti‐CD3 scFv effector domain. The Applicant 
performed studies to evaluate the kinetics of tebentafusp T cell redirecting capacity and to 
analyze the subsets of T cells that are activated. To determine the effects of tebentafusp on 
activation of specific T cell subsets, polychromatic flow cytometry was performed on T cells 
using gp100 peptide‐pulsed PBMCs as target cells. The phenotypic profile of CD8+ T cells 
activated with tebentafusp is shown in Figure 5 (tebentafusp at a concentration of ~10‐11 M in 
the presence of gp100 peptide‐pulsed PBMC target cells). Activated cells undergoing 
degranulation are depicted in green (CD107a staining). The major T cell populations activated in 
these experiments were the effector memory (TEM) and central memory (TCM) subsets as 
determined by CD45RO and CD27 staining.

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

37 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

The Applicant next assessed the threshold of epitope density necessary for tebentafusp‐
mediated T cell activation and whether the activity of tebentafusp was epitope density‐
dependent (Study IMC 1030). Briefly, the TCR domain of tebentafusp was tagged with biotin to 
allow a fluorescently labeled streptavidin to detect a single TCR‐peptide:HLA interaction. 
Microscopy was used to quantify the number of gp100 antigens presented on CM cell lines and 
T2 cells (a lymphoma‐derived cell line that expresses low levels of HLA‐A2) that had been 
exogenously pulsed with increasing levels of the target gp100 peptide. To determine the 
relationship between the level of gp100 antigen presentation on target cells and tebentafusp‐
mediated T cell activation, the T2 cells were pulsed with increasing concentrations of gp100 
antigen in the presence of 0.1 nM tebentafusp and CD 8+ T cells. IFNγ ELISPOT assays were then 
used to measure T cell activation.  
 
CM cell lines Mel526 and Mel624 exhibited an average of 33 epitopes and 30 epitopes, 
respectively (data not shown). In the T2 cells incubated with tebentafusp and the target gp100 
peptide epitope, IFNγ release was observed starting at ~100 pM peptide and reached its 
maximum between 1 and 10 nM of target gp100 peptide. These results suggest that T cell 
activation is correlated with epitope density. According to the Applicant, these results 
correspond to a minimum of ~5 epitopes per cell for T cell activation, with maximum activation 
observed beginning at a concentration of 10‐9 M gp100 peptide, corresponding to 
approximately 18 epitopes per cell.

Figure 8: Tebentafusp‐mediated T cell activation correlates with epitope density

(Source: Applicant figure reproduced from Study IMC 1030)

The Applicant performed a study to determine if the scFv portion of tebentafusp (UCHT1) binds 
to T cells in human, mouse, and monkey PBMCs (Study IMC 1029). Briefly, PBMCs were isolated 
from blood from human donors, cynomolgus monkeys, and mice (strain unspecified). Samples 
were stained with UCHT1‐his scFv protein, an IgG1 PE (isotype control), and species specific 
anti‐CD3 antibodies. Binding was assessed via flow cytometry. Comparison of staining of PBMCs 
from human, monkey, and mouse indicated that UCHT1 binds to CD3 on human T cells only 
(Figure 9).

|  |
|---|
| The Applicant next assessed the threshold of epitope density necessary for tebentafusp‐ |
| mediated T cell activation and whether the activity of tebentafusp was epitope density‐ |
| dependent (Study IMC 1030). Briefly, the TCR domain of tebentafusp was tagged with biotin to |
| allow a fluorescently labeled streptavidin to detect a single TCR‐peptide:HLA interaction. |
| Microscopy was used to quantify the number of gp100 antigens presented on CM cell lines and |
| T2 cells (a lymphoma‐derived cell line that expresses low levels of HLA‐A2) that had been |
| exogenously pulsed with increasing levels of the target gp100 peptide. To determine the |
| relationship between the level of gp100 antigen presentation on target cells and tebentafusp‐ |
| mediated T cell activation, the T2 cells were pulsed with increasing concentrations of gp100 |
| antigen in the presence of 0.1 nM tebentafusp and CD 8+ T cells. IFNγ ELISPOT assays were then |
| used to measure T cell activation. |
|  |
| CM cell lines Mel526 and Mel624 exhibited an average of 33 epitopes and 30 epitopes, |
| respectively (data not shown). In the T2 cells incubated with tebentafusp and the target gp100 |
| peptide epitope, IFNγ release was observed starting at ~100 pM peptide and reached its |
| maximum between 1 and 10 nM of target gp100 peptide. These results suggest that T cell |
| activation is correlated with epitope density. According to the Applicant, these results |
| correspond to a minimum of ~5 epitopes per cell for T cell activation, with maximum activation |
| observed beginning at a concentration of 10‐9 M gp100 peptide, corresponding to |
| approximately 18 epitopes per cell. |
|  |
| Figure 8: Tebentafusp‐mediated T cell activation correlates with epitope density |
|  |
| (Source: Applicant figure reproduced from Study IMC 1030) |
|  |
| The Applicant performed a study to determine if the scFv portion of tebentafusp (UCHT1) binds |
| to T cells in human, mouse, and monkey PBMCs (Study IMC 1029). Briefly, PBMCs were isolated |
| from blood from human donors, cynomolgus monkeys, and mice (strain unspecified). Samples |
| were stained with UCHT1‐his scFv protein, an IgG1 PE (isotype control), and species specific |
| anti‐CD3 antibodies. Binding was assessed via flow cytometry. Comparison of staining of PBMCs |
| from human, monkey, and mouse indicated that UCHT1 binds to CD3 on human T cells only |
| (Figure 9). |

In vivo studies 
 
To evaluate the anti‐cancer activity of tebentafusp , HLA‐A*02:01/gp100‐positive Mel526 
human melanoma cancer cells mixed with PBMCs isolated from human whole blood were 
engrafted subcutaneously into female Beige/severe combined immune deficiency (SCID) mice 
on Day 0 (Study CAM 018). On Days 1 to 5, tebentafusp was administered to the mice via 
intravenous tail injection once daily, at doses ranging from 0.004 to 0.1 mg/kg. Control animals 
received vehicle or a molecule that consisted of a TCR recognizing the HLA‐A*02:01 presenting 
an HIV‐GAG peptide fused to an anti‐CD3 scFv. Tumor size was assessed using caliper 
measurements three times weekly. As shown in Figure 10, tebentafusp inhibited tumor growth 
compared to controls.

Secondary Pharmacology

The Applicant’s Position:

There is no relevant species in which tebentafusp pharmacology can be tested. Therefore, extensive 
in vitro analysis of secondary pharmacodynamics was performed to characterize both on-
target/off-tumor and off-target/off-tumor effects of tebentafusp using a range of functional activity 
assays. The results of these can be summarized as follows:

 
Assessment in normal cells: In vitro assessments of redirected T cell activity were 
performed with tebentafusp in the presence of a range of normal cells from 13 tissue types. These 
studies showed no reactivity of tebentafusp against normal cells at ≤ 1 nM, except for epidermal 
melanocytes. Tebentafusp demonstrated reactivity against melanocyte lots at concentrations 
between 10 to 100 pM (10-100× MABEL). This activity was deemed to be on-target due to the 
confirmation of gp100 transcript and protein expression in the reactive cell lots, which is consistent 
with reports of gp100 expression in melanocytes. Given the low doses required to elicit an on-
target on-tumor response, tebentafusp showed a window between normal cells and the reference

| Figure 10: Tumor volume of tebentafusp treated SCID mice engrafted with human melanoma |
|---|
| cells and PBMCs |
|  |
| (Source: Applicant figure reproduced from Study CAM 018) |

| mg/kg, Group 5: 0.01 mg/kg, Group 6: 0.004 mg/kg, Group 7: Control molecule 0.04 mg/kg |

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

40 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

CM or UM cell line tested. This activity against melanocytes was not changed when tebentafusp 
was combined with durvalumab and/or tremelimumab. Tebentafusp demonstrated broad loss of 
specificity for the target pHLA complex from 1 nM (1,000× MABEL), where consistent evidence 
of T cell activation (IFNγ release) and/or redirected killing (GrB release) was observed across the 
different normal cells tested.

 
Alloreactivity: Most human TCRs only recognize and bind effectively to self-HLA 
molecules loaded with peptides. A small proportion (less than 10%) of TCRs have the ability to 
recognize non-self HLA molecules and can induce allorejection. This phenomenon is termed 
alloreactivity (Murphy, 2016). Tebentafusp was specifically engineered and selected for high 
affinity binding to the HLA-A*02:01-gp100 peptide complex. Therefore, binding to a non-HLA-
A*02 Class I molecule is unlikely to occur or, if it does, would occur at much reduced affinity than 
that for the target HLA-A*02 molecule. Alloreactivity was assessed by IFNγ in ELISPOT in a 
panel of Epstein-Barr virus-transformed B cell lines representing a large proportion of the target 
market Class 1 HLA genotypes, as targets, in the presence of tebentafusp. These studies were non-
GLP. These studies were non-GLP. Tebentafusp demonstrated no evidence of alloreactivity when 
tested against panels of cells displaying other Class I HLA types (inclusive of rare alleles).

 
Activity in whole blood and potential for cytokine release syndrome: The potential for 
cytokine release syndrome (CRS) was assessed in blood from 6 healthy HLA-A*02:01-positive 
donors incubated with increasing concentrations of tebentafusp, and samples were analyzed for 
evidence of cytokine release by multiplex. These studies were non-GLP. Tebentafusp did not show 
evidence for increased release of 5 key cytokines in the presence of whole blood at < 1 nM. 
Combining tebentafusp with durvalumab and/or tremelimumab did not enhance tebentafusp-
mediated cytokine release in whole blood.

 
Platelet activity: Given that platelets have been reported to have very high levels of HLA 
on their surface (Rowley, 2011; Chapman, 2012), they are a potential target for cross-reactivity of 
the TCR portion of tebentafusp. Since platelets play a key role in hemostasis, tebentafusp was 
examined in platelet-activation assays. Platelet activation assays were performed with whole blood 
from 6 healthy HLA-A*02:01-positive donors and samples were assessed by flow cytometry for 
the presence of the platelet activation marker CD62P. Additionally, to assess the ability of 
tebentafusp to interfere with blood-clotting, blood from one healthy HLA-A*02:01-positive donor 
was incubated with increasing concentrations of tebentafusp and the time to clotting was measured. 
These studies were non-GLP. Tebentafusp did not give any evidence of adversely affecting platelet 
activation or function. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment:

The Applicant performed a number of functional cross‐reactivity experiments to determine if 
there is a concentration of IMCgp100 at which a loss of target specificity is observed. Of note, at 
the recommended clinical dose, the steady‐state Cmax of IMCgp100 is 13 ng/mL and AUC0‐7d is 
4.6 ng∙day/mL.

| The FDA’s Assessment: |
|---|
| The Applicant performed a number of functional cross‐reactivity experiments to determine if |
| there is a concentration of IMCgp100 at which a loss of target specificity is observed. Of note, at |
| the recommended clinical dose, the steady‐state C of IMCgp100 is 13 ng/mL and AUC is max 0‐7d |
| 4.6 ng∙day/mL. |

Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

41 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

 Assessment in normal cells: To determine potential reactivity of tebentafusp, levels of

cytokine (IFNγ) or enzyme (GrB) released by redirected effector cells from PBMCs or 
purified CD8+ T cells were examined in a cell panel from 13 normal human tissues in the 
presence of increasing concentrations of tebentafusp up to 10 nM (Study IMC 1001‐IMC 
1013). FDA agrees with the Applicant’s position with respect to melanocyte findings. 
Additionally, tebentafusp showed activity at <1 nM in 1 of 5 donors when tested on 
human astrocytes (Study IMC 1001). The Applicant states: “Based on these [astrocyte 
and melanocyte] data, skin rash and potentially vitiligo were expected as the primary 
TCR mediated on‐target, off‐tumor toxicities.”

 Alloreactivity: FDA agrees. The tested concentrations were 1 nM tebentafusp (Study

IMC 1021), 200 pM (Study IMC 1057).

 Activity in whole blood and potential for cytokine release syndrome: In some samples,

IL‐6 , TNFα, and IFNγ were detectable in whole blood at concentrations of 10 and 100 
nM tebentafusp (Study IMC 1023). Levels were 2 to 10‐fold below the levels of cytokine 
release observed with positive controls.

 Platelet activity: FDA agrees.

 Impurity Assessment: IMCgp100 contains an identified active impurity (IMCgp100 +scFv

fragment), typically at a level of < 1% when tested across multiple batches. The 
Applicant performed in vitro experiments to assess the impact (Module 4.2.1.2; Study 
IMCgp100‐IMC 1058) on specificity of a batch of tebentafusp spiked with an artificially 
high (~6.7%) level of the IMCgp100 (+scFv fragment) impurity. Briefly, GMP reference 
standard batch of tebentafusp was compared with the spiked sample and assessed for 
specificity by screening for redirected T cell activity by IFNγ release (IFNγ ELISpot) with 
antigen positive and antigen negative normal cells derived from high risk tissue types 
and for cytokine release in whole blood (Study IMC 1058).

Higher levels of IFNγ and TNFα release were detected at 0.2 – 1 nM of the tebentafusp 
spiked sample when compared to non‐spiked tebentafusp. This data indicates that high 
levels of impurity may alter the activity of tebentafusp.

### Safety Pharmacology

The Applicant’s Position:

Tebentafusp can only recognize human HLA; therefore, all nonclinical toxicology studies were 
conducted in vitro.  Therefore, no specific safety pharmacology studies were conducted. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
FDA agrees.

#### ADME/PK

The Applicant’s Position:

Tebentafusp can only recognize human HLA; therefore, all nonclinical toxicology studies were 
conducted in vitro.  Therefore, conventional in vivo animal models used to evaluate drug 
distribution, bioavailability, mechanism(s) of elimination, and potential risks of specific tissue 
toxicity are considered limited. Nevertheless, the nonclinical pharmacokinetics (PK) of 
tebentafusp were evaluated in four animal studies: non tumor-bearing and tumor-bearing severe 
combined immune deficiency (SCID) mice, C57BL/6 mice, and CD57 or HDD mice.  These 
studies were non-GLP, PK and /or biodistribution studies and administered as single intravenous 
(IV) or subcutaneous (SC) dose.

Preclinical biodistribution studies demonstrate that tebentafusp is being retained in the melanoma 
tumor for a significantly longer timeframe compared to other non-target tissues, with a half-life of 
tumour retention of approximately 24 hours.

Observed pharmacokinetics for tebentafusp after IV administration were generally linear across 
dose levels tested in different strains of mice.  Maximum concentrations (Cmax) and exposure 
(AUC) increased in an approximately dose proportional manner for dose levels from 0.005 to 0.05 
mg/kg.  The maximum concentrations were recorded at 15 minutes, as well as a secondary 
concentration peak at 1 hour, while the radioactivity was detectable in all samples up to 72 hours. 
The terminal half-lives calculated from the plasma radioactivity concentrations were also similar 
at each dose level. Tebentafusp exposure was maintained at measurable levels for 72 hours. 
Following SC administration of [3H]-tebentafusp to CD57 mice at 0.05 mg/kg, the maximum mean 
plasma radioactivity concentration was lower and measured later than that measured after the IV 
dose route at the same dose level, as expected due to the dose route. Concentrations then decreased 
and were still detectable in all samples at 72 hours. The terminal half-live calculated from the 
plasma radioactivity concentrations was similar to that calculated following the intravenous 
administration. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment:

#### Toxicology

#### General Toxicology

The Applicant’s Position:

Tebentafusp can only recognize human HLA; therefore, all nonclinical toxicology studies were 
conducted in vitro. Human tissue and cell-based in vitro approaches were taken to understand 
potential organ-specific toxicities that may arise in the clinical setting with tebentafusp. These 
approaches considered the on-target/off-tumor potential effects, as well as the off-target/off-tumor 
effects (cross-reactivity) of tebentafusp. These studies are detailed in Secondary Pharmacology 
Section above.

[Complex Table: page 44 — 17 rows × 6 cols]

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

44 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

Tebentafusp was also investigated in toxicology studies to assess its cross‐reactivity with 
normal tissues in a GLP‐compliant tissue cross‐reactivity study. The ability of tebentafusp to 
bind to a panel of 35 normal human tissues from 3 healthy donors was assessed using 
immunohistochemistry (IHC). Staining with tebentafusp was observed in the membrane and 
cytoplasm of lymphocytes throughout the human tissue panel examined. Similar staining was 
observed with the individual anti‐CD3 component of tebentafusp, while no staining was 
observed with the individual TCR component, indicating that this staining was attributable to 
the binding of tebentafusp to CD3. As CD3 is a T lymphocyte marker, this staining was expected. 
No other staining was observed across the panel of tissues tested, indicating no unexpected 
cross‐reactivity. It should be noted that the TCR targeting domain of tebentafusp recognizes 
very low target pHLA complexes (>5 pHLA complexes per cell), which are expected to be 
outside the normal range of sensitivity of the IHC method and would therefore inform on cross‐
reactivity above the threshold of detection of IHC only. Overall, the results of this study are 
consistent with published reports of gp100 being specific to melanocytes and melanoma cells, 
and not in other. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
In general, and as noted by the Applicant, the use of IHC has limitations based on the low 
number of target complexes (i.e., IHC is not as sensitive a measure for this cross‐reactivity 
assessment). Tebentafusp staining was observed in the membrane and cytoplasm of 
lymphocytes in the mammary gland, gastrointestinal tract (colon, esophagus, small intestine, 
and stomach), kidney, liver, lung, lymph node, fallopian tube, prostate, salivary gland, skin, 
spleen, thymus, thyroid, tonsil, ureter, urinary bladder, and cervix (Study IM 1861). FDA agrees 
that this appears to be attributable to CD3 binding. Of note, brain was negative for staining, 
indicating tebentafusp activity at <1nM in 1/5 human astrocyte samples is likely of low clinical 
risk (Study IMC 1001).

#### Genetic Toxicology

The Applicant’s Position:

Tebentafusp can only recognize human HLA; therefore, all nonclinical toxicology studies were 
conducted in vitro.  Therefore, no genetic toxicology studies were conducted.  The indication is 
for patients with unresectable or metastatic uveal melanoma whose life expectancy is limited 
and the risk of death from their uveal melanoma outweighs the risk.  This is consistent with 
requirements of ICH 6, and ICH 9. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
Based on the biologic properties of tebentafusp, a fusion protein, genetic toxicology studies are 
not warranted.

| The FDA’s Assessment: |
|---|
| In general, and as noted by the Applicant, the use of IHC has limitations based on the low |
| number of target complexes (i.e., IHC is not as sensitive a measure for this cross‐reactivity |
| assessment). Tebentafusp staining was observed in the membrane and cytoplasm of |
| lymphocytes in the mammary gland, gastrointestinal tract (colon, esophagus, small intestine, |
| and stomach), kidney, liver, lung, lymph node, fallopian tube, prostate, salivary gland, skin, |
| spleen, thymus, thyroid, tonsil, ureter, urinary bladder, and cervix (Study IM 1861). FDA agrees |
| that this appears to be attributable to CD3 binding. Of note, brain was negative for staining, |
| indicating tebentafusp activity at <1nM in 1/5 human astrocyte samples is likely of low clinical |
| risk (Study IMC 1001). |

| The FDA’s Assessment: |
|---|
| Based on the biologic properties of tebentafusp, a fusion protein, genetic toxicology studies are |
| not warranted. |

Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

#### Carcinogenicity

The Applicant’s Position:

Tebentafusp can only recognize human HLA; therefore, all nonclinical toxicology studies were 
conducted in vitro.  Therefore, no carcinogenicity toxicology studies were conducted.  The 
indication is for patients with unresectable or metastatic uveal melanoma whose life 
expectancy is limited and the risk of death from their uveal melanoma outweighs the risk. This 
is consistent with requirements of ICH 6, ICH 9, and S1. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
Carcinogenicity studies were not warranted for the proposed indication.

#### Reproductive and Developmental Toxicology

The Applicant’s Position:

Tebentafusp is a bispecific fusion protein for the treatment of unresectable or metastatic uveal 
melanoma. The targeting end is an affinity-enhanced T cell receptor (TCR) that binds a human 
peptide fragment of the melanoma associated antigen glycoprotein 100 (gp100) presented by 
human leukocyte antigen-A*02:01 (HLA-A*02:01), which is expressed on the surface of 
melanoma cells. The effector end is a single chain variable fragment (scFv) that binds 
specifically to human CD3 on T cells. Once administered, tebentafusp is expected to bind to 
melanoma tissue and stimulate the immune system to attack the target tissue. Due to the 
specificity for human targets, embryo-fetal development (EFD) studies cannot be conducted in 
nonclinical species. Therefore, a weight of evidence assessment was conducted to evaluate the 
risk of tebentafusp administration on pregnancy and embryo-fetal development. A literature 
search revealed that gp100 is expressed embryonically in melanocytes found in the skin, inner 
ear, and eyes by 7 weeks of gestation, and MHC Class I classical proteins (including HLA-
A*02:01) and transporter-associated-with-antigen-processing (TAP) proteins required for 
presentation of antigen on the cell surface are expressed very early in embryonic development.  
In addition, CD3-positive T cells begin to populate the periphery and are capable of being 
activated by around 12 weeks of gestation.

However, tebentafusp’s ability to directly affect embryo-fetal development requires that 
tebentafusp crosses the placenta and gains access to the fetus.  The molecular weight of 
tebentafusp is 77 kDa, therefore, it is much too large to cross the placenta by diffusion. It also 
does not contain an Fc domain and, therefore, cannot bind to the neonatal Fc receptor (FcRn), 
which facilitates transfer of IgG molecules across the placenta, nor does it have domains that 
could bind to other placental transporters. Thus, tebentafusp would not be able to gain access to 
or elicit pharmacologic activity directly in the embryo/fetus. There is also no evidence that

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

46 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

tebentafusp could interfere with implantation or the maintenance of pregnancy through its 
mechanism of action.

Based on the results of this weight of evidence assessment, tebentafusp would not be expected to 
adversely affect embryo-fetal development or the maintenance of pregnancy when administered 
during pregnancy. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
Biologics of this molecular weight may cross the placenta. Based on its mechanism of action, 
tebentafusp may cause fetal harm, particularly the skin, inner ear, and eye, if the developing 
fetus is exposed to tebentafusp.

#### Other Toxicology Studies

The Applicant’s Position: 
None conducted.  
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
No additional toxicology studies were warranted.

### 6 Clinical Pharmacology

## Executive Summary

Tebentafusp is a bispecific gp100 peptide‐HLA‐A*02:01 directed CD3 T cell engager. The 
applicant is seeking approval of tebentafusp for the treatment of HLA‐A*02:01‐positive adult 
patients with unresectable or metastatic uveal melanoma. The proposed dosing regimen is 20 
mcg on Day 1, 30 mcg on Day 8, 68 mcg on Day 15, and 68 mcg once every week thereafter, via 
intravenous infusion over 15‐20 minutes.

The primary efficacy and safety evidence supporting the proposed dosage regimen results from 
the pivotal Phase 2 study IMCgp100‐202, a randomized, open‐label trial demonstrating 
improvements in overall survival (median of 21.7 months for Tebentafusp arm vs 16 month for 
Investigator’s choice arm, with HR of 0.73 and p‐value of 0.0139) and an overall acceptable 
safety profile in Tebentafusp arm.

The dose selection is supported by the results from study IMCgp100‐102 and study IMCgp100‐
01. Study IMCgp100‐01 suggested that the flat dosing is preferable given more adverse events 
(AEs) observed in patients with higher body weight receiving body weight based dosing. Study 
IMCgp100‐01 also showed no clinically meaningful differences in safety or efficacy between the 
weekly and daily dosing regimens. The maintenance dose selection is supported by the results 
from study IMCgp100‐102, in which a higher 73 mcg dose was not well tolerated as indicated by 
elevations in hepatic transaminases with concurrent low‐grade increases in bilirubin. No dose‐
limiting toxicities nor significant liver enzyme elevations were observed at the maintenance 
dose of 68 mcg.

Cytokine release syndrome (CRS) is one of the most common adverse reactions, occurred in 
89% of patients in Study IMCgp100‐202 who received tebentafusp. CRS led to permanent 
treatment discontinuation in 1.2% of patients. The priming dosing strategy (20 mcg on Day 1, 
30 mcg on Day 8) was adopted to mitigate CRS.

Treatment‐emergent anti‐drug antibodies (ADA) against tebentafusp were detected in 33% and 
29% of patients receiving tebentafusp across all doses in study IMCgp100‐102 and study 
IMCgp100‐202, respectively. The median onset time to ADA formation was 6‐9 weeks after 
tebentafusp treatment. Tebentafusp concentration decreased in patients with ADA positivity . 
Ratio of tebentafusp concentration of ADA+/ ADA‐ at cycles 5‐13 ranged between 0.01 to 0.5. 
High‐titer (>Median titer of 8192) ADAs increased tebentafusp clearance by 35‐fold. Exploratory 
subgroup analysis does not show evidence of clinically meaningful ADA impact on efficacy or 
safety. Information regarding the presence and impact by neutralizing ADA (Nab) on 
pharmacokinetics (PK), safety and efficacy was not included in current submission. A Post

## Labeling

Information regarding the presence and impact by neutralizing anti‐drug antibodies (Nab) on 
pharmacokinetics (PK), safety and efficacy was not included in the current submission. This 
following PMC is to be issued to request assessing the presence of Nab in all patient samples 
that tested positive for binding antibodies and evaluating its impact on PK, safety and efficacy.

Conduct an assessment to determine the presence of neutralizing anti‐drug antibodies 
against tebentafusp in all patient samples that tested positive for binding anti‐drug 
antibodies in studies IMCgp100‐202 and IMCgp100‐102 by using the validated 
neutralizing anti‐drug antibody assay. Assess the clinical impact of the neutralizing anti‐
drug antibodies on pharmacokinetics, efficacy and safety of tebentafusp in the final 
report. Include datasets and analyses in the final study report. 
 
The applicant proposed final report submission date is February 2022.

The Applicant’s Position:

Noncompartmental analysis of tebentafusp from study IMCgp100-102 in mUM patients 
indicated dose-proportional PK with a terminal t1/2 of 6.8 to 7.5 hours. The estimated systemic 
CL and central volume of distribution of tebentafusp in melanoma patients were 4.33 L/d and 
5.25 L, respectively. In population PK modeling, the eGFR and high-titer ADA (≥ median titer) 
were identified as significant covariates impacting CL.

Exposure-response analysis revealed there were no clinically significant trends between 
tebentafusp exposure and key safety or efficacy endpoints over the dose range investigated (QW 
IV infusion of 5 ng/kg to 68 µg of tebentafusp). In the final population PK model tebentafusp CL 
slightly decreased with decreasing renal function. However, CL values in renally impaired 
patients were essentially within the range observed in patients with normal renal function. 
Additionally, no impact on safety or efficacy parameters was identified in patients with mild to 
moderate renal impairment. Therefore, no dose adjustment is recommended in renally impaired 
patients. There is no information available in patients with severe renal impairment.

The FDA’s Assessment: 
 
FDA agrees with the sponsor’s position. The tebentafusp clinical pharmacology program 
includes three clinical studies (Table 7). The results from these three studies generally provide 
adequate characterization of tebentafusp PK properties, exposure‐response relationships and 
immunogenicity assessment. The Applicant’s population PK (PPK) model is generally acceptable 
with minor change to the definition of immunogenicity. FDA’s position is that ADA status should 
be treated as time‐varying covariates. Please refer to section 19.5.4 for detail. Impact of ADA

The Applicant’s Position:

The recommended dosing regimen of tebentafusp is 20 mcg on Day 1, 30 mcg on Day 8, 68 mcg 
on Day 15, and 68 mcg once every week thereafter. Tebentafusp is administered via IV infusion 
over 15 to 20 minutes. The selection of intra-patient dose escalation over the first 3 weeks was

BEST AVAILABLE COPY

BEST AVAILABLE COPY

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

56 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

identified in Study 01 and the actual doses were selected during the Phase 1 portion of 
Study 102. The final, recommended dosing regimen was then studied in the Phase 2 portion of 
Study 102 and the Phase 3 Study 202.

Study 01: Several key observations in the first-in-human, Phase 1 study (Study 01) led to 
recommending an intra-patient escalation regimen using fixed doses. First, in Study 01, patients 
with higher body weights who received higher absolute doses of tebentafusp experienced more 
severe treatment-related toxicity. Consequently, fixed doses, rather than weight-based doses, 
were incorporated during the study. Second, given no clinically meaningful differences in safety 
or efficacy were identified between the weekly and daily dosing regimens, and in light of the 
easier utility of a weekly regimen, subsequent evaluation focused on weekly dosing. Third, the 
emerging safety profile of tebentafusp suggested that CRS (and associated hypotension) was 
more likely to occur during the first 2 weeks, after which tachyphylaxis occurred. Therefore, as 
has been used for other T cell-engaging bispecifics, an intra-patient escalation regimen during 
Weeks 1 and 2 (ie, 20 and 30 mcg, respectively) prior to dosing 50 mcg was selected as the 
preferred regimen to evaluate further in the subsequent single-arm, Phase 1/2 Study 102.

Study 102: In Study 102, the intra-patient escalation regimen based on fixed dosing identified in 
Study 01 was further optimized by evaluating higher Week 3 doses (54 to 73 mcg) to achieve 
greater exposure. During the Phase 1 portion of this study, which employed a standard 3+3 dose-
escalation design, the 73 mcg dose level was not tolerated due to elevations in hepatic 
transaminases with concurrent low-grade increases in bilirubin in 2 of 4 treated patients. These 
hepatic events were reversible without administration of corticosteroids, and all patients who 
experienced dose-limiting toxicities remained on treatment. After a dose reduction to 68 mcg, 
6 patients were treated with no dose-limiting toxicities and no significant liver enzyme 
elevations. As a result, a dose of 68 mcg was identified as the maximum tolerated dose and 
selected for further evaluation as part of the intra-patient escalation regimen during the Phase 2 
portion of the study.

Study 202: The efficacy and safety of the intra-patient escalation regimen from Study 102, ie, 20 
mcg on Day 1 and 30 mcg on Day 8, followed by the escalated dose of 68 mcg on Day 15 and 
weekly thereafter, were confirmed in the Phase 3 Study 202 in which all patients randomly 
allocated to tebentafusp received this regimen. In Study 202, this tebentafusp dosing regimen 
delivered convincing OS benefit and statistically significant PFS, and acceptable safety with a 
low discontinuation rate and no treatment-related deaths. Additionally, data from Study 102 
revealed pharmacodynamic biomarkers consistent with the MoA including cytokine induction 
and T cell trafficking into the tumor.

Blood samples were taken for PK assessment in patients at pre-dose-, the end of infusion, and 
12 - 24 hours post end of infusion for each QW dose for the first cycle, pre-dose and at the end of 
infusion on the first dose of each cycle thereafter, and at the End of Treatment (EOT) visit.

PK parameters calculated by noncompartmental methods from the Phase 1 dose escalation cohorts 
after dosing on C1D1 were comparable due to the dose range evaluated. Serum concentrations 
were measurable up to 24 hours after dosing but were below the LLOQ at subsequent timepoints

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

57 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

between 24 and 168 hours. The Cmax was achieved immediately after the end of infusion (median 
0.5 hours), with a geometric mean Cmax of 4063 pg/mL for C1D1 (20 mcg dose; Figure 11) and 
13040 pg/mL for C1D15 (68 mcg dose; Figure 12). The increase in geometric mean PK exposure 
(Cmax) of tebentafusp was approximately dose proportional between the 20 mcg dose administered 
on C1D1 and the 68 mcg dose administered on C1D15. From the dose escalation data, the observed 
terminal t1/2 was 6.8 to 7.5 hours. Subsequent mean Cmax values after C1D15 ranged from 10000 
to 20000 pg/mL, and mean pre-dose concentrations were undetectable, indicating no accumulation 
with QW dosing at the 68 mcg dose (Figure 13). 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
 
FDA agrees with the Applicant’s position that the proposed dosing regimen of tebentafusp for 
the general patient population ( 20 mcg on Day 1, 30 mcg on Day 8, 68 mcg on Day 15, and 68 
mcg once every week thereafter, refer to as 20/30/68 mcg ) is acceptable. The dosing regimen 
selection is based on the following rationale:

 Priming dosing regimen: Cytokine release syndrome (CRS) mainly occurs within the first

3 weeks of dosing. 
 Weekly dosing regimen: No clinically meaningful differences in safety or efficacy were

identified between the weekly and daily dosing regimens. The weekly dosing regimen 
was adopted given the easier utility. 
 Flat dosing regimen: Patients with higher body weights who received higher absolute

doses of tebentafusp experienced more severe treatment‐related toxicity. Analysis of PK 
data indicated that tebentafusp Cmax is directly proportional to the absolute dose. As a 
result, heavier patients tended to have higher serum IMCgp100 concentrations. The 
data thus indicated that heavier patients were being unduly exposed to risk, which 
warranted a move from weight dosing to flat dosing. 
 Maintenance dose of 68 mcg: 73 mcg was not well tolerated (elevations in hepatic

transaminases with concurrent low‐grade increases in bilirubin); furthermore, there 
were no dose‐limiting toxicities and no significant liver enzyme elevations at the 
selected dosing regimen (20/30/68 mcg) in study 102 (N=6).

Although only one priming dosing regimen (20 mcg on Day 1, 30 mcg on Day 8) was evaluated, 
the FDA considers further priming dose optimization effort is not necessary or feasible based on 
the following information:

 The rate of grade 3+ CRS is low (0‐0.5%).  
 Grade 2 CRS was transient and mainly occurred during the first 3 weeks of dosing. 
 Small number of patients (n=3) were treated with tocilizumab to mitigate CRS. 
 The observed low ORR of 9% posts challenge for further priming dose optimization.

Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

61 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

The Applicant’s Position:

Effects on the PK of tebentafusp by the baseline characteristics were visually assessed by 
comparing the distributions of the interindividual variability estimates from the base PK model 
with tebentafusp. Baseline characteristics included, but were not limited to, age, weight, body 
mass index, eGFR, renal function, ADA titer, maximum ADA titer, ADA status, sex, race, 
melanoma stage, sum of tumor diameters, percent change from baseline sum of tumor diameters, 
and largest liver lesion group. Visual inspection of covariate versus eta (inter-individual 
variability) plots demonstrated that baseline renal function (eGFR) and ADA (titer>8192) were 
the only statistically meaningful covariates on final PK model fit.

Estimated tebentafusp CL slightly decreased (3.32 L/d) in patients with moderate renal 
impairment (eGFR ranging from 30 to 59 mL/min; Figure 14 ,Table 6). High interpatient 
variability was observed (78 %CV) and CL values in renal impaired patients were essentially 
within the range observed in patients with normal renal function. No impact on safety or efficacy 
parameters was identified in patients with mild to moderate renal impairment and no dose 
adjustments are recommended. There is no information available in patients with severe renal 
impairment (eGFR less than 30 mL/min).

Estimated CL considerably increased when an ADA titer greater than 8192 was present (Figure 
15; Table 6 ). However, ADA status was not associated with safety or efficacy and thus, 
monitoring of ADA is not necessary.

Assessment of goodness of fit plots (Figure 16) was conducted to evaluate the performance of 
the final PK model. The model diagnostic plots indicate that the final PK model adequately 
describes the observed concentration data. No absolute value of conditional weighted residual 
error was greater than 4, and no recorded concentrations, were excluded based on poor fitting in 
model development.

The PK of tebentafusp in patients with melanoma enrolled in these trials was adequately 
described by a two-compartment model with a first-order elimination from the central 
compartment. Only eGFR and the presence of high titer ADA were identified as relevant PK 
covariates. The estimated CL slightly decreased in patients with moderate renal impairment. 
However, CL values in renal impaired patients were essentially within the range observed in 
patients with normal renal function. Additionally, no impact on safety or efficacy parameters was 
identified. Therefore, no dose adjustment is recommended in renally impaired patients. 
Tebentafusp exposure was considerably decreased when an ADA titer greater than 8192 was 
present. Because ADA status was not associated with safety or efficacy of tebentafusp, 
monitoring of ADA is not necessary. Age, sex, weight, and race had no impact on the PK of 
tebentafusp in patients with melanoma.

No dose modification are required for intrinsic or extrinsic factors.

The FDA’s Assessment: 
 
FDA agrees with the Applicant’s position that no dose adjustment is necessary for patients with 
mild and moderate renal impairment although the estimated CL slightly decreased in this 
subpopulation. 
 
The tebentafusp clearance increased in patients with high titer ADAs. There was clear evidence 
of time‐dependent impact of ADA on PK at the end of the infusion after Cycle 5. Mean Ratio of 
geometric mean tebentafusp concentration in ADA+/ ADA‐ patients during Cycle 6‐13 in study 
102 ranged between 0.01‐0.55 and during cycle 5‐13 for study 202 ranged between 0.01‐0.5 
(Figure 17). ADA status should be treated as time‐varying covariates. Please refer to section 
19.5.4 for details. The impact of ADA Titer >=8192 on exposure was re‐estimated and the USPI 
was updated as described in section 12.3. The figures (Figure 15, Figure 48) visualizing the ADA 
effect on PK provided by the applicant treated ADA status or titer as a fixed factor; therefore, 
FDA does not agree with the applicant’s presentation of these data.

#### Comprehensive Clinical Pharmacology Review

#### General Pharmacology and Pharmacokinetic Characteristics

Data:

#### Lymphocyte Re-distribution

As anticipated based on other T-cell engagers, blood lymphocyte levels decreased by 60% within 
24 hours of the first dose of tebentafusp and returned to baseline by Day 8 after the first dose 
(Figure 19). This pattern was repeated after the third dose of tebentafusp on Day 15. Samples 
were not collected after the second dose (Day 8). These results are consistent with activation of 
lymphocytes followed by their margination and re-distribution into tumors and tissues.

#### Figure 19

### 1.1.

Consistent with other T cell engagers, all patients exhibited a treatment-induced increase in all 
cytokines and chemokines measured after the first dose. Levels of 11 immune markers associated 
with inflammatory responses (IFN-γ, tumor necrosis factor [TNF]-α, IL-2, and IL-6), immune 
modulation (IL-10 and IL-1RA), and chemotaxis (C-X-C motif ligand [CXCL]9[MIG], CXCL10 
[IP-10], CXCL11[I-TAC], hepatocyte growth factor (HGF), and monocyte chemoattractant

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

protein-1 [MCP-1]) were measured in patient serum samples collected at baseline and on-
treatment.

Serum biomarker changes were transient, peaking at 8 to 24 hours post treatment. Biomarkers 
returned to baseline levels as measured in samples collected at Day 8, prior to patients receiving 
the second dose. The treatment-induced increase was seen again after the third dose, where the 
magnitude of increase was higher for certain markers, notably IL-10 and CXCL10 (Figure 20). 
Samples were not analyzed after the second dose (Day 8).

#### Figure 20

CXCL = C-X-C motif Ligand; D = Day; HGF = hepatocyte growth factor; IL = interleukin; MCP = monocyte 
chemoattractant protein; TNF – tumor necrosis factor

### 1.2.

Consistent with other T-cell engagers, increases in CD3 (median 3.2-fold; Figure 21), CD8 
(median 2.3-fold; Figure 22) and CD4 (median 2.1-fold; Figure 23) T cells within the tumor 
microenvironment were observed 16 days after the first dose.  Biopsies were collected prior to 
treatment initiation and 16 days post first dose, (i.e., after the third dose) and analyzed for CD3, 
CD4 and CD8 T cells by immunohistochemistry.  The majority of biopsies (68%) had a 
≥1.5 -fold increase in CD3 T cells by Day 16, after 3 doses of tebentafusp.

### 1.3.

As shown in Figure 24, consistent with the gp100-dependent activation of T cells by tebentafusp, 
gene expression analyses of paired baseline and on-treatment tumor biopsies collected at Day 16 
revealed the following increases in messenger RNA (mRNA) levels after 3 doses of tebentafusp:

 Interferon alpha (type 1 IFN) and gamma (type 2 IFN) pathway genes (2- and 3-fold 
respectively)

 HLA class 1, transporter associated with antigen processing (TAP)1, TAP2 and

Proteasome 20S Subunit Beta 8 (PSMB8) (2-fold).

  Cytotoxic CD8 genes, granzyme B and perforin, that are required for tumor cell killing

(2- to 3-fold).
