#### Study 102 Results

This section will focus mainly on the results from the Phase 2 expansion part of Study 102, 
which evaluated the proposed dosing regimen of tebentafusp.

## Compliance with Good Clinical Practices

Data: 
Not applicable. 
 
The Applicant’s Position:

The study was conducted in accordance with the protocol, the ethical principles derived from 
international guidelines including the Declaration of Helsinki and Council for International 
Organizations of Medical Sciences ethical guidelines, applicable ICH GCP guidelines, and 
applicable laws and regulations, as described in the following sections of Protocol Version 8: 
 Ethical consideration and administrative procedures: Protocol Section 11 
 Data handling and management: Protocol Section 10 
 Study drug compliance and accountability: Protocol Section 6.8.1

Immunocore Clinical Quality Assurance group or designee conducted audits at 2 study centers. 
No critical audit findings were observed. For all audit findings, appropriate corrective and 
preventive actions were undertaken.

## Financial Disclosure

Data: 
Not applicable. 
 
The Applicant’s Position:

## Financial disclosures for Study 102 are addressed in Section 19.2 of this assessment aid.

### Patient Disposition

[Complex Table: page 130 — 6 rows × 7 cols]

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

130 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

Data: 
Patient disposition is summarized in Figure 34 for the Phase 1 dose escalation and Phase 2 dose 
expansion cohorts. The DCO date for this study was 20 March 2020.

Phase 1 dose escalation cohorts: Of the 21 patients screened, 19 patients were enrolled into 
4 cohorts: 3 in Cohort 1 54 mcg, 6 in Cohort 2 64 mcg, 4 in Cohort 3 73 mcg, and 6 in Cohort 4 
68 mcg. All 19 (100%) enrolled patients received study drug, of whom 18 (94.7%) patients 
discontinued study drug and 1 (5.3%) patient remained on treatment. At the DCO date, the 
duration of exposure for the patient who remains on treatment was 42.1 months. The primary 
reason for treatment discontinuation was disease progression (89.5%). At the data cut-off date, a 
total of 5 (26.3%) patients remained on study and 14 (73.7%) patients were reported to have 
ended the study (all due to death).

Phase 2 dose expansion cohort: Of the 148 patients screened, 127 patients were enrolled into 
the Phase 2 dose expansion part of the study. All 127 (100%) patients received study drug, of 
whom 106 (83.5%) patients discontinued study drug and 21 (16.5%) patients remained on 
treatment. The primary reason for treatment discontinuation was disease progression (70.1%). At 
the data cut-off date, 53 (41.7%) patients remained on study and 74 (58.3%) patients were 
reported to have ended the study. Death was the primary cause of study discontinuation (54.3%).

The Applicant’s Position:

The causes of study treatment discontinuation were generally consistent with the disease under 
study.

#### Protocol Violations/Deviations

Data: 
In the Phase 2 dose expansion cohort, the most common important protocol deviations were 
related to informed consent and trial procedures (7.9% each) (Table 27). These patients were 
re-consented using the incorrect version of the ICFs, re-consenting was not done in a timely 
manner, and fresh tumor biopsy samples were not collected in a timely manner.

One patient was excluded from the PPS as the patient did not complete post-treatment scans 
required per-protocol until PD. The patient refused to have post-treatment scans (but did not 
formally withdraw consent).

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

133 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

The Applicant’s Position:

Overall, the deviations identified as important are consistent with the execution of a Phase 1/2 
clinical study with an intensive schedule of assessments, frequent dosing, and visits. Given the 
number of patients enrolled, and volume of data collected over the study duration, the deviations 
were determined to not impact the interpretation of the study results as they pertain to the study’s 
relevant objectives and endpoints. Moreover, the COVID-19 pandemic had no substantial impact 
on the assessment of the study objectives.

Protocol deviations were managed according to the protocol deviation guidance provided by the 
Sponsor and were reviewed monthly by the Sponsor.

#### Table of Demographic Characteristics

Data: 
In the Phase 2 dose expansion cohort, the majority of patients were White (99.3%) and 
approximately half were female (50.7%), with a mean age of 60.4 years (range, 25 to 88 years) 
(Table 28). There was a higher proportion of patients who were <65 years of age (63.0%) versus 
>65 years of age (37.0%). The majority of patients (70.1%) had an ECOG performance status 
score of 0 at baseline. A total of 58.3% of patients had baseline LDH >ULN, 80.3% of patients 
had baseline ALC ≥1.0 × 109/L, and 70.9% of patients had baseline ALP ≤ULN.

The demographic characteristics of the patients enrolled in Study 102 were generally consistent 
with the target mUM population.

Other Baseline Characteristics (e.g., disease characteristics, important concomitant drugs)

Data: 
Baseline disease characteristics 
For most patients in the Phase 2 expansion cohort, the choroid was the initial UM site (75.7%) 
and metastasis was not observed at initial diagnosis (92.3%) (Table 29). Approximately one-third 
of patients had LDH > ULN 250 U/L (36.0%), a poor prognostic factor for advanced UM, and 
more than half of the patients’ largest metastatic lesion recorded at baseline was ≤ 3 cm (55.3%). 
For the majority of patients (79.9%), the pre-randomization choice of therapy was 
pembrolizumab. Most patients did not receive prior surgery for metastatic disease (91.3%).

Time since primary diagnosis/first metastasis to start of study is calculated relative to treatment start date. Partial primary

Source: Module 5.3.5.2, Study 102, Table 14.1.5. 
 
Prior medications 
All Phase 2 dose expansion patients received at least 1 prior anticancer therapy in the metastatic 
setting, two-thirds of patients received 1 line of prior anticancer therapy, and one-third of 
patients received at least 2 lines of prior anticancer therapy. The most commonly reported prior 
anticancer therapies were systemic therapies (83.5%, of which immunotherapy was reported in 
73.2% of patients), radiotherapy (75.6%), LDT (44.9%), and surgery (40.9%). Forty-three 
(33.9%) patients received ≥2 lines of prior anticancer therapy. The most commonly reported 
prior anticancer therapy regimen was radiotherapy (77.2%). 
 
The Applicant’s Position:

The disease characteristics and prior therapies of the mUM population enrolled in Study 102 
were generally consistent with the target mUM population of pretreated patients with poor 
prognostic indicators.

#### Treatment Compliance, Concomitant Medications, and Rescue Medication Use

Data: 
Data are presented below as part of the applicant’s position. 
 
The Applicant’s Position:

Treatment compliance 
In the Phase 2 expansion cohort, the median relative dose intensity was 100.0% (range, 62.6% to 
100%) for tebentafusp. 
 
Concomitant medications 
The most commonly reported concomitant medications were paracetamol (89.0%) and IV 
sodium chloride (52.0%).

### Efficacy Results – Primary Endpoint (Including Sensitivity Analyses)

Data: 
Objective response rate (RECIST v1.1) assessed by ICR 
Objective response rate per RECIST v1.1 according to ICR for the FAS is summarized in

Table 30. Of the 127 patients who received study drug, an OR of PR was observed in 6 (4.7%) 
patients (95% CI: 1.8%, 10.0%).

A minor response was defined as a reduction from baseline in the sum of longest diameters or short axis of target lesions (mm) 
of 10% to 29%, where non‐target lesion response was not unequivocal progression, and no new lesions were present. 
Confirmation is required after ≥4 weeks. 
The sub‐categories under stable disease were not mutually exclusive categories. 
Percentages were based on the number of patients in the Full Analysis Set. 
Tumor assessment was based on RECIST v1.1 by ICR.

Sensitivity analysis 
Objective response rate per RECIST v1.1 according to ICR for the PPS. Of the 126 patients who 
received the study drug, an OR of PR was observed in 6 (4.8%) patients (95% CI: 1.8%, 10.0%). 
These results were similar to those from the primary analysis of ORR for the FAS. 
 
The Applicant’s Position:

The primary endpoint of ORR per RECIST v1.1 according to ICR, as evaluated in the Phase 2 
dose expansion cohort, was 4.7%. Results from the sensitivity analyses (4.8%) were similar to 
those from the primary analysis of ORR for the FAS.

### Efficacy Results – Secondary and other relevant endpoints

Data: 
Disease control rate 
Of the 127 patients in the Phase 2 expansion cohort, 57 (44.9%) patients had SD at ≥8 weeks 
per RECIST v1.1 according to ICR (

Table 30). The DCR was 31.5% (40 patients; 95% CI: 23.5%, 40.3%) at ≥16 weeks and 22.8% 
(29 patients; 95% CI: 15.9%, 31.1%) at ≥24 weeks. 
 
Duration of Response 
Among patients with an objective response in the Phase 2 expansion cohort, the median duration 
of response was 8.7 months (95% CI: 5.6, 24.5) (Table 31). The estimated percentage of ongoing 
responders was 60.0% (95% CI: 12.6%, 88.2%) at 6 months and 20.0% (95% CI: 0.8%, 58.2%) 
at 12 months. Results were similar for the FAS and PPS.

CI = confidence interval; ICR = Independent Central Review; N = total number of patients; NC = not calculable. 
[a] Events were patients with a date of disease progression or death in the absence of disease progression, following the first 
documented objective response (CR or PR). Patients without disease progression or death, were censored at the last evaluable 
tumor assessment. Response confirmation is required ≥4 weeks later following initial evaluation for complete and partial 
response. 
[b] Percentage of patients in response were estimated using the Kaplan‐Meier method. 
Only patients who had a confirmed objective response (complete response or partial response) were included. 
Tumor assessment was based on RECIST v1.1 by ICR. 
Source: Module 5.3.5.2, Study 102 CSR, Table 14.2.1.1.2.
 
Time to response 
Among patients with an objective response in the Phase 2 expansion cohort, the mean (Std) time 
to response was 7.0 months (6.9), with response times ranging from 1.6 to 20.5 months. 
 
Rate and duration of minor response 
In the Phase 2 expansion cohort, the rate of MinR or better (CR, PR, or MinR) was 11.0% (14 of 
127 patients; 95% CI: 6.2%, 17.8%). The median duration of MinR or better was 10.3 months 
(95% CI: 5.8, 23.2). The estimated percentage of responders (including those with a MinR) was 
78.6% (95% CI: 47.2%, 92.5%) at 6 months and 33.3% (95% CI: 10.9%, 58.0%) at 12 months. 
 
Evaluation of tumor size 
Tumor size was presented graphically using a waterfall plot, to present each patient’s best 
percent change from baseline in tumor size and BOR as assessed by RECIST v1.1 by ICR for the 
Phase 2 dose expansion cohort in Figure 35. Overall, the waterfall plots illustrate benefit beyond 
traditional PR defined by RECIST v1.1.

Figure 35 
Study 102: Tumor Size – Best Percent Change from Baseline Waterfall Plot 
(RECIST v1.1 by Independent Central Review) ‐ Phase 2 Dose Expansion

Treatment group: Phase 2 dose expansion cohort (N=127, n=119)

+ Patient received prior immunotherapy. 
Tumor size was measured as the sum of longest diameters or short axis of the target lesions according to RECIST v1.1 by 
Independent Central Review. 
Best percent change in target lesion size was the maximum percent reduction from baseline or the minimum percent increase 
from baseline (in the absence of a reduction), up until disease progression or starting subsequent alternative cancer therapy.  
Complete response, partial response or minor response required confirmation at least 4 weeks later.  
Reference lines at 20%, ‐10%, and ‐30% mark target lesion response criteria for disease progression, minor response, and 
partial response, respectively.  
Only patients with at least one evaluable postbaseline target lesion scan were included. Eleven patients overall were not 
included due to non‐measurable disease at baseline or no evaluable postbaseline target lesion scans. Evaluable postbaseline 
scans must be on or prior to disease progression or starting subsequent alternative cancer therapy to be considered. 
Source: Module 5.3.5.2, Study 102 CSR, Figure 14.2.5.1. 
 
In addition, a spider plot was produced to display the percent change from baseline in tumor size 
over time (Figure 36). Overall, the spider plots demonstrated unique patterns of radiographic 
changes. Notably, some patients exhibited rapid decreases in tumor burden at the first disease 
assessment at 6 weeks, whereas others had more gradual decreases. For the majority of 
responders, responses were durable.

Progression-free survival 
In the Phase 2 expansion cohort, the median PFS was 2.8 months (95% CI: 2.0%, 3.7%) (Figure 
37). Of the 127 patients in the Phase 2 dose expansion cohort, the estimated PFS rates were 
25.0% (95% CI: 17.8%, 32.9%) at 6 months and 10.9% (95% CI: 6.2%, 17.2%) at 12 months. A 
total of 117 (92.1%) patients had the event of disease progression (106 [83.5%] patients) or death 
in the absence of disease progression (11 [8.7%] patients).

Events were either disease progression or death in the absence of disease progression, which occurred within 2 tumor 
assessment visits of the last evaluable assessment. Events that did not occur within 2 tumor assessment visits were censored.

Source: Module 5.3.5.2, Study 102 CSR, Figure 14.2.2.3. 
 
Overall survival 
In the Phase 2 expansion cohort, the median duration of follow-up was 19.6 months (95% CI: 
16.0, 22.2). The median OS was 16.8 months (95% CI: 12.9, 21.3) (Figure 38). Sixty-nine 
patients died and 58 patients were censored for any reason. Of the 127 patients in the Phase 2 
dose expansion cohort, the OS rates were 61.8% (95% CI: 52.6%, 69.8%) at 12 months and 
37.0% (95% CI: 26.5%, 47.5%) at 24 months.

The Applicant’s Position:

Together, these findings indicate that responses were durable and patients derived benefit beyond 
the traditional PR defined by RECIST.

Tebentafusp demonstrated encouraging survival benefit. After a median follow-up of 
19.6 months, the median OS was 16.8 months. Although this was a single-arm study, when 
compared with historical data (median OS < 12 months; Khoja, 2019), the OS results are 
promising in mUM patients, many of whom were heavily pretreated and had poor prognostic 
factors.

### Efficacy Results – Secondary or exploratory COA (PRO) endpoints

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

146 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

Data: 
Health‐related quality of life 
Health-related quality-of-life data were collected using the EORTC QLQ-C30 and EQ-5D,5L 
PRO instruments.

EORTC-QLQ-C30 
There was no deterioration (based on a threshold of 10 points) at Cycle 1, Cycle 5, or Cycle 9 
across all dimensions: physical functioning, role functioning, cognitive functioning, and social 
functioning, nausea, vomiting, pain, dyspnea, insomnia, appetite loss, constipation, diarrhea, 
financial difficulties, and global health status/quality of life.

EQ-5D, 5L 
The EQ-5D-5L questionnaire compliance results were 75.3% at baseline (expected, 93 according 
to protocol version 7 or later, of which 70 evaluable), 78.7% at C3D1, 80.4% at C5D1, and 
82.1% on C9D1.

Mean EQ-5D index and EQ-VAS scores at baseline were 0.8109 and 77.2, respectively, and not 
significantly different to that of the general population norms (0.779 for index and 77.8 for 
EQ-VAS). There was no deterioration in either EQ-5D or EQ-VAS at Cycle 1, Cycle 5, or Cycle 
9, or at the EOT (based on MID= 0.075 or 7.5, respectively) (Szende, 2014). 
 
The Applicant’s Position:

Health-related quality of life, as assessed by the EORTC-QLQ-C30 and EQ-5D,5L PRO 
instruments, showed no deterioration in patients treated with tebentafusp.

The FDA’s Assessment: 
 
FDA has reviewed the Applicant’s description of the conduct of Study 102, including compliance 
with GCP, patient characteristics and disposition, treatment compliance and concomitant 
medications, data quality and integrity along with the Applicant’s analyses of primary, 
secondary, and exploratory endpoints; however, as this is a supportive study, FDA did not verify 
the analyses and results are considered exploratory.  
 
FDA considers the analysis of PFS and OS in Study 102 to be descriptive in nature, as results of

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

147 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

these endpoints in a single‐arm study are uninterpretable. Similarly, FDA notes that no 
conclusions should be drawn from PRO analyses in a single arm study.  
 
The observed response rate was 5% in Study 102, creating significant uncertainties with respect 
to risk‐benefit based on the single arm trial. Accordingly, randomized Study 202 was necessary 
to assess whether tebentafusp provided benefit to patients, especially given the potential for 
life‐threatening cytokine release syndrome.

#### Integrated Review of Effectiveness

The FDA’s Assessment: 
Study 202 is the primary study supporting the efficacy claim for this BLA.  Study 102 was 
submitted as a supportive study. Study 202 and Study 102 were evaluated separately, and no 
integrated review of effectiveness was performed.

#### Assessment of Efficacy Across Trials

When comparing the efficacy results between Phase 3 Study 202 and the Study 102 Phase 2 
expansion, key differences between the 2 studies, should be considered. Briefly, these include 
the following: (1) Study 202 enrolled patients who had not received prior systemic therapy in 
the metastatic setting (1L), whereas Study 102 Phase 2 expansion enrolled patients who were 
previously treated in the metastatic setting (2L+); (2) Study 202 included a control group 
(investigator’s choice) whereas Study 102 was a single‐arm study; and (3) the primary endpoint 
was OS in Study 202 and ORR in Study 102 Phase 2 expansion (ORR and OS were secondary 
endpoints in Study 202 and Study 102, respectively).

### Primary Endpoints

Data: 
Overall survival 
Table 32 summarizes OS in Phase 3 Study 202 and Study 102 Phase 2 expansion.

In Study 202, based on a median follow‐up duration of 14.1 months and 150 death events in 
the ITT Analysis Set, the primary endpoint of OS significantly favored tebentafusp with a HR of 
0.51 (95% CI: 0.37, 0.71; p < 0.0001) (Table 20 and Figure 27 above). Although not yet mature, 
the Kaplan‐Meier estimates suggest median OS was significantly prolonged in the tebentafusp 
arm compared with the investigator’s choice arm (21.7 vs 16.0 months). Kaplan‐Meier 
estimates of OS at 12 and 24 months were 73.2% and 44.8%, respectively, for tebentafusp, and 
58.5% and 20.3%, respectively, for investigator’s choice.

In Study 102 Phase 2 expansion, based on a median follow‐up duration of 19.6 months and 
69 death events in the FAS, the median OS was 16.8 months (Figure 38 above). Kaplan‐Meier

The Applicant’s Position:

OS observed in Study 102 Phase 2 expansion in HLA‐A*02:01‐positive patients with previously 
treated mUM. 
 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
FDA’s assessment of efficacy is based primarily on Study 202. FDA considered Study 102 
separately only as a supportive trial. Comparisons between the trials were not performed due 
to the limitations of cross trial comparisons. In addition, event endpoints like OS and PFS are 
uninterpretable in single arm trials.

| The FDA’s Assessment: |
|---|
| FDA’s assessment of efficacy is based primarily on Study 202. FDA considered Study 102 |
| separately only as a supportive trial. Comparisons between the trials were not performed due |
| to the limitations of cross trial comparisons. In addition, event endpoints like OS and PFS are |
| uninterpretable in single arm trials. |

Data: 
Progression‐free survival 
Table 33 summarizes PFS in Phase 3 Study 202 and Study 102 Phase 2 expansion.

In Study 202, based on a median follow‐up duration of 11.4 months and 295 PD or death 
events, PFS favored tebentafusp and met statistical significance (HR 0.73; 95% CI: 0.58, 0.94; 
p = 0.0139) (Table 21 and Figure 28 above). Median PFS was 3.3 months in the tebentafusp arm 
and 2.9 months in the investigator’s choice arm, and 6‐month PFS rates were 30.9% and 18.9%, 
respectively.

In Study 102 Phase 2 expansion, based on a median follow‐up duration of 25.8 months and 
117 PD or death events, the median PFS was 2.8 months and 6‐month PFS rate was 25.0% 
(Figure 37).

Tumor response 
Table 34 summarizes tumor response in Phase 3 Study 202 and Study 102 Phase 2 expansion.

In Study 202, ORR (investigator‐reported) was numerically higher with tebentafusp than 
investigator’s choice (9.1% vs 4.8%) (Table 34). In the tebentafusp arm, BORs were 1 CR (0.4%), 
22 PRs (8.7%), 92 SDs (36.5%), and 131 PDs (52.0%). In the investigator’s choice arm, there 
were no CRs; 6 patients (4.8%) had PRs, 28 (22.2%) had SD, and 78 (61.9%) had PD. Disease 
control rate (defined as CR + PR + SD ≥ 12 weeks) favored tebentafusp compared with

The Applicant’s Position:

Tebentafusp demonstrated favorable PFS in both Study 202 and Study 102 Phase 2 expansion 
and rather with a statistically significant benefit over investigator’s choice in patients with mUM 
who had not received prior systemic therapy in the metastatic setting in Study 202.

Tumor response rates were modest in both Study 202 and Study 102 Phase 2 expansion. 
However, the response rate was numerically higher for tebentafusp compared with 
investigator’s choice in patients with mUM who had not received prior systemic therapy in the 
metastatic setting in Study 202. Across both studies, RECIST based endpoints consistently 
underestimated the OS benefit from tebentafusp.

The FDA’s Assessment: 
Comparisons between the trials were not performed due to the limitations of cross trial 
comparisons. In addition, event endpoints like OS and PFS are uninterpretable in single arm 
trials. 
 
FDA agrees that ORR in Study 202 was not consistent with the observed OS and does not 
appear to be an appropriate endpoint to predict the OS benefit seen in the tebentafusp arm.

Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

The Applicant’s Position:

Overall, the OS benefit demonstrated with tebentafusp in Study 202 was generally observed 
across all prespecified major demographic and known prognostic subgroups. In Study 102 
Phase 2 expansion, the trends in median OS and 1-year OS for each subgroup was generally 
similar to those observed in the tebentafusp arm of Study 202. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
While FDA does not object to the Applicant’s presentation of the results of the OS analysis in 
Studies 202 and 102, FDA cautions against cross‐study comparisons due to limitations inherent 
in such comparisons  and the potential impact of differences between the studies.  
 
FDA agrees with the Applicant’s position that the OS benefit seen in Study 202 is consistent 
across the subgroups analyzed. The greatest difference in HR for OS is observed in subgroup 
analysis based on LDH, which was a stratification factor for Study 202. In Study 202, the HR for 
OS is 0.35 (95% CI: 0.21, 0.60) in patients with LDH ≤ 250 U/L and 0.70 (95% CI: 0.46, 1.09) in 
patients with LDH > 250 U/L . However, FDA considers all subgroup analyses as exploratory 
only.

#### Additional Efficacy Considerations

The FDA’s Assessment: 
None

#### Integrated Assessment of Effectiveness

Data: 
Data are presented below as part of the applicant’s position. 
 
The Applicant’s Position:

The efficacy of tebentafusp is clearly established in mUM. In the pivotal Phase 3 Study 202 in 
HLA-A*02:01-positive patients with mUM who had not received prior systemic therapy in the 
metastatic setting, tebentafusp demonstrated a significant improvement in OS, reducing the 
relative risk of death by 49% compared with investigator’s choice of either pembrolizumab, 
ipilimumab, or dacarbazine. The estimated 1-year OS rate was 73.2% for tebentafusp and 58.5% 
for investigator’s choice. The superior tebentafusp OS benefit was generally observed across all 
subgroups analyzed, including patients with poor prognostic factors. These data confirm the 
promising OS observed in the supportive Study 102 Phase 2 expansion in HLA-A*02:01-
positive patients with previously treated mUM.

| The FDA’s Assessment: |
|---|
| While FDA does not object to the Applicant’s presentation of the results of the OS analysis in |
| Studies 202 and 102, FDA cautions against cross‐study comparisons due to limitations inherent |
| in such comparisons and the potential impact of differences between the studies. |
|  |
| FDA agrees with the Applicant’s position that the OS benefit seen in Study 202 is consistent |
| across the subgroups analyzed. The greatest difference in HR for OS is observed in subgroup |
| analysis based on LDH, which was a stratification factor for Study 202. In Study 202, the HR for |
| OS is 0.35 (95% CI: 0.21, 0.60) in patients with LDH ≤ 250 U/L and 0.70 (95% CI: 0.46, 1.09) in |
| patients with LDH > 250 U/L . However, FDA considers all subgroup analyses as exploratory |
| only. |

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

157 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

Tebentafusp significantly improved PFS, reducing the relative risk of disease progression or 
death by 27%, compared with investigator’s choice. Moreover, both DCR at 12 weeks (45.6% vs 
27.0%) and the point estimate for ORR (9.1% vs 4.8%) were higher with tebentafusp than 
investigator’s choice. As observed for other immunotherapies, RECIST response rate 
underestimated the OS benefit from tebentafusp.

While Study 202 was not designed to identify the contribution of treatment beyond initial 
progression, investigators were permitted by the protocol to allow for treatment beyond initial 
progression for tebentafusp and pembrolizumab. At the time of the DCO, just under half (43.3%) 
of patients in the tebentafusp arm were treated beyond initial progression; 21.5% of the total 
tebentafusp treatment administered during the study was delivered after the initial tumor 
progression.  Based on a landmark OS analysis from Study 202 in patients with BOR of PD, 
tebentafusp provides a dramatic OS benefit, resulting in a 57% reduction in the relative risk of 
death over investigator’s choice. These data suggest that a best response of PD is not a sign of 
lack of benefit and should not be the sole reason for discontinuation of tebentafusp treatment.

Together, tebentafusp showed a highly significant OS benefit versus investigator’s choice in the 
Phase 3 Study 202 in HLA-A*02:01-positive patients with mUM who had not received prior 
systemic therapy in the metastatic setting. The control arm was generally consistent with 
historical reported trials confirming that tebentafusp provides substantial improvement over 
available therapies (Rantala, 2019; Pelster, 2021; Piulats, 2021). Tebentafusp is the first systemic 
treatment to demonstrate a statistically significant and clinically meaningful survival benefit in 
mUM, representing an essential treatment option for patients with this serious and life-
threatening disease. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
The analysis of effectiveness of tebentafusp was based on data submitted from 378 patients 
enrolled to Study 202. FDA agrees that tebentafusp demonstrated a statistically significant and 
clinically meaningful improvement in OS over investigator choice for patients with HLA‐
A*02:01‐positive metastatic uveal melanoma who had not received prior systemic therapy in 
the metastatic setting (HR of 0.51 [95% CI: 0.37, 0.71], p < 0.0001). The results of Study 202 
support full approval for this indication.   
 
FDA does not agree with the Applicant’s analysis of treatment past progression, and no 
conclusions regarding benefit of continuing treatment with tebentafusp after evidence of 
progressive disease can be drawn.

### Safety Review Approach

The Applicant’s Position:

Two pools of safety data will be considered: Pool 1 comprising UM patients (Uveal 
Melanoma Patients) and Pool 2 comprising all melanoma patients who received tebentafusp as 
monotherapy (All Melanoma Monotherapy Patients).   
 
 
Pool 1 (Uveal Melanoma Patients): Safety data will be reported side by side for UM 
patients from Study 01 (including rollover Study 401 data), Study 102 dose-escalation patients 
(Phase 1), Study 102 dose-expansion patients (Phase 2), Study 202 (tebentafusp arm only), 
pooled data for the tebentafusp arms of Study 102 (dose escalation and expansion) 
and Study 202, and pooled data for all studies. The uveal melanoma pool (n=410) provides 
primary evidence of safety and tolerability of tebentafusp monotherapy in the target metastatic 
uveal melanoma patient population regardless of line of therapy, where the randomized Study 
202 (n=245) provides the majority of the safety data.  
  
 
Pool 2 (All Melanoma Monotherapy Patients): Safety data will be reported side by 
side for Study 01 (including rollover Study 401 data), Study 102 (combining dose escalation and 
expansion), Study 202 (tebentafusp arm only), monotherapy patients from Study 201, and pooled 
data for all studies. A supportive assessment of safety and tolerability of tebentafusp 
monotherapy is provided in the all melanoma pool (N=505). This safety dataset allows 
characterization of the safety profile of tebentafusp as a monotherapy, in patients across tumor 
types and regardless of line of therapy. The pool will be used to assess any difference in profile 
due to adding of cutaneous melanoma patients. Table 37 shows the number of patients from each 
study included in Pools 1 and 2.  
 
Table 37: Pooled Safety Datasets

Cycle 1 Day 1, 30 mcg at Cycle 1 Day 8, and 68 mcg at Cycle 1 Day 15 and weekly thereafter.

When comparing the two pools of safety data, there is consistency of results between UM and all 
melanoma pool. Therefore, the primary presentation in this document will be done using the UM 
pool of patients.

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

161 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

Overall, tebentafusp has a well-established safety profile across the 5 Immunocore-sponsored 
clinical studies in 587 patients with melanoma, including 410 patients with UM, who were 
exposed to tebentafusp monotherapy. The randomized Study 202 provides the primary safety 
data and is proposed to be the basis for labeling presentation of Warning and Precautions and 
Adverse Events sections of the USPI. While the Study 102 safety data are consistent with Study 
202 and supportive of the proposed indication and dosing regimen.  
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
The primary data used by FDA to assess and characterize the safety of tebentafusp at the 
proposed recommended dosage (20 mcg on Day 1, 30 mcg on Day 8, 68 mcg on Day 15, and 68 
mcg once every week thereafter until disease progression, unacceptable toxicity, or death) is 
derived from the pivotal trial, Study 202, in which 245 patients received at least 1 dose of 
tebentafusp and 111 patients received at least 1 dose of investigator’s choice agent.  
 
Patients enrolled to Study 202 were required to be 18 years old or older. Key exclusion criteria 
for this study impacting interpretation of safety data include:

 Serum creatinine > 1.5 X ULN and/or creatinine clearance (calculating using Cockcroft‐

Gault formula, or measured) < 50 mL/minute 
 Total bilirubin > 1.5 × ULN, except for patients with Gilbert’s syndrome who are

excluded if total bilirubin > 3.0 × ULN or direct bilirubin > 1.5 × ULN 
 Alanine aminotransferase > 3 × ULN, Aspartate aminotransferase > 3 × ULN 
 Absolute neutrophil count < 1.0 × 109/L, Absolute lymphocyte count < 0.5 × 109/L 
 Platelet count < 75 × 109/L, Hemoglobin < 8 g/dL 
 Clinically significant cardiac disease or impaired cardiac function (NYHA Grade ≥2, QTcF

> 470 msec, acute myocardial infarction or unstable angina pectoris < 6 months prior to 
screening.  
 Known history of HIV infection 
 Active HBV or HCV infection 
 History of adrenal insufficiency, interstitial lung disease, pneumonitis that required

corticosteroid treatment or current pneumonitis, history of colitis of inflammatory 
bowel disease 
 
The protocol included dose modifications for tebentafusp for the following toxicities:

 Skin Toxicity Pruritis:

o For Grade 3 events, hold tebentafusp until ≤ Grade 1 
 Rash/Photosensitivity

o For Grade 2 and 3 events, hold tebentafusp until ≤ Grade 1 
o For Grade 4 events, permanently discontinue tebentafusp 
 Hypotension

o For Grade 4 events, dose reduce or permanently discontinue tebentafusp

|---|
| clinical studies in 587 patients with melanoma, including 410 patients with UM, who were |
| exposed to tebentafusp monotherapy. The randomized Study 202 provides the primary safety |
| data and is proposed to be the basis for labeling presentation of Warning and Precautions and |
| Adverse Events sections of the USPI. While the Study 102 safety data are consistent with Study |
| 202 and supportive of the proposed indication and dosing regimen. |

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

162 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

 Infusion‐related Reactions/Anaphylaxis (occurring during infusion)

o For Grade 3 events that do not improve by at least 1 grade within 6 hours with

medical management or Grade 4 toxicity, permanently discontinue tebentafusp 
 Infusion‐related Reactions (occurring after infusion/Cytokine Release Syndrome (based

on Lee 2014  grading criteria)

o For Grade 3 events, hold tebentafusp until resolution of symptoms and restart

after discussion and written approval of Sponsor’s Medical Monitor 
o For Grade 4 events, permanently discontinue tebentafusp 
 Hepatic Function Abnormalities

o For Grade 3 or 4 events, hold tebentafusp until ≤ Grade 1 
 Vomiting

o For Grade 3 or 4 events, hold tebentafusp until ≤ Grade 1 
 Other Adverse Events:

o For Grade ≥ 3 events, hold study drug until Grade 1.

The protocol‐specified dose reductions of tebentafusp for toxicity is as follows: from a starting 
tebentafusp dose of 68 mcg, the dose will be reduced to 54 mcg for any toxicity requiring dose 
reduction. The dose may be reduced further to 50 mcg for recurrent toxicity. Patients who 
require more than 2 dose reductions of tebentafusp should discontinue treatment. 
 
To confirm the Applicant’s safety signals identified in Study 202 and to identify any additional, 
rare, but potentially serious safety signals, FDA reviewed the Applicant’s analyses for Pool 1 and 
separately analyzed Applicant Pool 2 which comprised 505 patients with uveal or cutaneous 
melanoma who received at least 1 dose of tebentafusp at any dose level. For FDA’s analyses, 
Applicant Pool 2 is referred to as ISS_Pool. The analysis of the ISS_Pool is summarized in Section 
8.2.11. In its safety analysis, FDA did not consider safety analyses of individual studies other 
than the pivotal trial Study 202. 
 
FDA calculated the frequency of all treatment emergency adverse events (TEAEs) at the PT, HLT, 
HLGT, SOC MedDRA levels as well as using standard narrow, broad, and algorithmic SMQs, and 
FDA Office of Oncologic Disease (OOD) defined grouped terms (GT) for Study 202 and the 
ISS_Pool. The FDA defined group terms are a set of related preferred term and are defined in 
Appendix 19.3. Use of a grouped term is denoted by (GT) following the group name e.g., 
Fatigue (GT) comprises the preferred terms fatigue and asthenia. In its analysis of TEAEs, FDA 
considered all reported events regardless of investigator assessment of relatedness.  
 
Analysis of the adverse reaction of cytokine release syndrome (CRS) is discussed in detail along 
with the other adverse events of special interest (AESIs), which include skin reaction/rash and 
hepatotoxicity, in Section  8.2.5. 
 
For safety assessments based on laboratory and vital signs, FDA considered the objective data

| To confirm the Applicant’s safety signals identified in Study 202 and to identify any additional, |
| rare, but potentially serious safety signals, FDA reviewed the Applicant’s analyses for Pool 1 and |
| separately analyzed Applicant Pool 2 which comprised 505 patients with uveal or cutaneous |
| melanoma who received at least 1 dose of tebentafusp at any dose level. For FDA’s analyses, |
| Applicant Pool 2 is referred to as ISS_Pool. The analysis of the ISS_Pool is summarized in Section |
| 8.2.11. In its safety analysis, FDA did not consider safety analyses of individual studies other |
| than the pivotal trial Study 202. |
|  |
| FDA calculated the frequency of all treatment emergency adverse events (TEAEs) at the PT, HLT, |
| HLGT, SOC MedDRA levels as well as using standard narrow, broad, and algorithmic SMQs, and |
| FDA Office of Oncologic Disease (OOD) defined grouped terms (GT) for Study 202 and the |
| ISS_Pool. The FDA defined group terms are a set of related preferred term and are defined in |
| Appendix 19.3. Use of a grouped term is denoted by (GT) following the group name e.g., |
| Fatigue (GT) comprises the preferred terms fatigue and asthenia. In its analysis of TEAEs, FDA |
| considered all reported events regardless of investigator assessment of relatedness. |
|  |
| Analysis of the adverse reaction of cytokine release syndrome (CRS) is discussed in detail along |
| with the other adverse events of special interest (AESIs), which include skin reaction/rash and |
| hepatotoxicity, in Section 8.2.5. |
|  |
| For safety assessments based on laboratory and vital signs, FDA considered the objective data |

rather than adverse event reporting. In general, FDA has not included TEAE reporting for 
laboratory investigations in TEAE frequency tables (i.e., in labeling).

## Review of the Safety Database

#### Overall Exposure

Data: 
The clinical development program of tebentafusp in melanoma is comprised of 5 Immunocore 
sponsored clinical studies, including 2 completed studies (Phase 1, IMCgp100-01 [Study 01]; 
Phase 2 rollover study IMCgp100401 [Study 401]) and 3 ongoing studies (Phase 1/2, 
IMCgp100-102 [Study 102]; Phase 1b/2, IMCgp100-201 [Study 201]; and Phase 3, IMCgp100-
202 [Study 202]) (Table 38).

The Applicant’s Position:

Across the clinical studies presented in Table 38, 587 patients with melanoma, including 410 
patients with uveal melanoma (UM), have been exposed to tebentafusp monotherapy. Study 202 
contributes both primary efficacy and safety data for the proposed indication. Studies 102 and 01 
contribute both supportive efficacy and safety data for the proposed indication. Studies 201 and 
401 contribute safety data for the proposed indication.  
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
 
The Applicant’s table, Table 38, summarizes the number of patients across the development 
program that have received tebentafusp. Table 39 summarizes the exposure for patients in the

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

169 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

The Applicant’s Position:

Demographics and baseline characteristics were generally similar across studies with some 
differences in baseline characteristics (i.e., elevated lactate dehydrogenase [LDH], largest liver 
lesion diameter, time from initial diagnosis to study treatment) related to first line (1L) vs. 
second line or greater (2L+) population.

Overall, approximately half of the patients were male (51%), with a median age of 62 years 
(range 23 to 91 years). More than half of the patients were between 18 and < 65 years (57.3%) 
and 42.7% of patients were ≥ 65 years of age. The majority of patients (75.4%) had an ECOG 
performance status score of 0 (fully active). Approximately one‐third of patients had an initial 
diagnosis of stage II disease and nearly one‐quarter of patients had a stage III diagnosis. The 
vast majority of patients (95.4%) had a liver lesion, 48.5% of which were < 3 cm and 46.8% of 
which were ≥ 3 cm. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
 
Table 41 summarizes the key demographic characteristics for the safety analysis set of Study 
202. Overall, the two arms were well balanced with respect to gender, age, ECOG performance 
status, race, and ethnicity. FDA notes that although uveal melanoma occurs much less 
frequently in Black/African American, Asian, and American Indian/Alaskan Native patients, 
these populations appeared to be underrepresented in Study 202.

#### Adequacy of the safety database:

Based on the incidence of metastatic uveal melanoma in the US and globally, a safety database 
of 410 uveal melanoma patients treated with tebentafusp is representative and considered 
adequate to determine the overall safety profile of tebentafusp.   The integrated safety findings 
are consistent with the safety observed in the randomized Study IMCgp100-202, which is the 
study proposed as the primary basis for labeling of safety in the USPI. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment:  
The size of the safety database is adequate to provide a reasonable estimate of adverse 
reactions that may be observed with tebentafusp at the proposed dosage; duration of the 
treatment is adequate to allow assessment of adverse reactions over time. The safety database 
represents the gender, age, and race consistent with that observed in the overall population of 
patients who are diagnosed with uveal melanoma. However, the lack of data for patients who 
identify as Black/African American, Asian and American Indian/Alaskan Native may limit the 
generalizability of conclusions to these populations.

#### Adequacy of Applicant’s Clinical Safety Assessments

| treatment is adequate to allow assessment of adverse reactions over time. The safety database |
| represents the gender, age, and race consistent with that observed in the overall population of |
| patients who are diagnosed with uveal melanoma. However, the lack of data for patients who |
| identify as Black/African American, Asian and American Indian/Alaskan Native may limit the |
| generalizability of conclusions to these populations. |

Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the

#### Issues Regarding Data Integrity and Submission Quality

The Applicant’s Position:

The rationale for integrating safety data was to provide an assessment of the overall safety 
profile of tebentafusp and to increase precision around the likely incidence of rare events. 
However, differences in study design, including eligibility criteria and dosing regimens, should 
be considered when interpreting the pooled safety data. Given the key design differences among 
the studies, integrated safety tables will present the individual study results as well as the pooled 
results. No tables will present pooled results only.  
 
The FDA’s Assessment:

FDA performed the following analyses to assess the quality and consistency of the safety data:

## 1. Comparison of coding: The FDA reviewer compared the coding of the actual reported

term (AETERM) to the lowest level term in the MedDRA hierarchy (AELLT) for the 9448 
treatment emergent adverse events (TEAEs) that were reported for patients enrolled on 
Study 202. Overall the coding was consistent and appropriate. The reporting of Cytokine 
Release Syndrome by investigators likely underrepresents the incidence. This is 
discussed further in Section 8.2.5. This does not represent an issue with data integrity or 
a coding error. 
2. Comparison of treatment discontinuations due to adverse events as reported in the

subject level ADSL, which includes patient disposition, and as reported in ADAE variable 
AECN (action taken) did not identify significant discrepancies. 
3. Comparison of dose delays and modifications due to adverse events as reported in the

exposure data file and as reported in adverse events did not identify significant 
discrepancies. 
4. Comparison of assessment of relatedness to study treatment for fatalities and serious

### adverse events based on evaluation of narratives did not identify significant

in the label.

Data:

Categorization of AEs are described below.

The Applicant’s Position:

For the integrated analyses, AEs were coded using the Medical Dictionary for Regulatory

| 1. Comparison of coding: The FDA reviewer compared the coding of the actual reported |

| term (AETERM) to the lowest level term in the MedDRA hierarchy (AELLT) for the 9448 |

| Study 202. Overall the coding was consistent and appropriate. The reporting of Cytokine |
| Release Syndrome by investigators likely underrepresents the incidence. This is |
| discussed further in Section 8.2.5. This does not represent an issue with data integrity or |
| a coding error. |
| 2. Comparison of treatment discontinuations due to adverse events as reported in the |

| in the label. |

Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

172 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

Activities (MedDRA) Version 23.1 or later and graded based on the National Cancer Institute 
Common Terminology Criteria for Adverse Events (NCI CTCAE) Version 4.03. Data from 
studies originally reported in previous versions of MedDRA were recoded to the most current 
version for the integrated summary of safety (ISS) database.

For pooling of TEAE data, the incidence of each preferred term (PT) and system organ class 
(SOC) was pooled across the relevant studies based on the original individual study reporting 
and derivations using the Analysis Data Model datasets. A TEAE was defined as any AE that 
occurred on or after the date of initial receipt of study drug. The analysis of AEs (as describe in 
this SCS) was limited to TEAEs. Any AEs considered to be non-treatment emergent are 
presented in the individual CSRs only. All TEAEs were summarized descriptively by MedDRA 
SOC and PT count with (n) and percentage (%). 
 
Based on data from ongoing and completed tebentafusp clinical studies, the following adverse 
events of special interest (AESI) have been specified: CRS, acute skin reactions, and LFT 
elevations. Other events of interest include cardiac and eye disorders were identified in Studies 
202 and 102. These events are discussed further in Section 8.2.5. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
FDA agrees.

#### Routine Clinical Tests

The Applicant’s Position:

The SeCore® HLA Sequencing System and uTYPE® Dx v1.0 HLA Sequence Analysis Software 
developed by Thermo Fisher Scientific have been used as a companion diagnostic (CDx) to 
identify HLA-A*02:01 positive patients for tebentafusp clinical studies. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
In Study 202, hematology and chemistry laboratory assessment values were graded for severity 
according to NCI‐CTCAE 4.03. The schedule for vital sign and laboratory assessments depended 
on the treatment to be administered. For patients randomized to receive tebentafusp, vital 
signs were performed before and after dosing according to protocol and institutional standards 
(minimum every 4 hours for 16 hours C1D1, C1D8, C1D15). Laboratory assessments 
(hematology and chemistry panels) were assessed weekly prior to each dose. Patients were also 
evaluated for safety events with physical examination and adverse event monitoring.

### Safety Results

#### Deaths

| The FDA’s Assessment: |

| In Study 202, hematology and chemistry laboratory assessment values were graded for severity |
| according to NCI‐CTCAE 4.03. The schedule for vital sign and laboratory assessments depended |

| signs were performed before and after dosing according to protocol and institutional standards |
| (minimum every 4 hours for 16 hours C1D1, C1D8, C1D15). Laboratory assessments |
| (hematology and chemistry panels) were assessed weekly prior to each dose. Patients were also |
| evaluated for safety events with physical examination and adverse event monitoring. |

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

174 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

Across the tebentafusp clinical development program, the vast majority of deaths were due to 
disease progression. None of the deaths were considered to be related to tebentafusp by the 
investigator.

Across all studies, a total 173 patients (42.2%) died. The most common cause of death was 
disease progression in 165 patients (40.2%); followed by deaths due to “other” reasons in 4 
patients (1%); two patients (0.5%) who died due to “unknown” reasons; and two patients (0.5%) 
who died due to adverse events. None of these were considered related to tebentafusp.

Among the 4 deaths where the primary cause was due to “other” reasons, the free text 
descriptions in the CRF included the following: “fell downstairs and had stroke” 163 days after 
tebentafusp was discontinued due to disease progression [Study 102, Patient 
], “clinical 
disease progression” [Study 102, Patient 
] and “unknown” for two patients in Study 202 
[Patient 
 and Patient 
]. Patient 
 died 235 days after tebentafusp was 
discontinued due to disease progression, while the death for Patient 
 occurred 27 days 
after the last tebentafusp dose and was due to metastatic uveal melanoma and unrelated to 
tebentafusp per investigator.

The two deaths where the primary cause was due to “unknown” reasons occurred in Study 01 
[Patient 
 and Patient 
] 143 and 173 days, respectively, after treatment with 
tebentafusp was discontinued due to disease progression.

The two deaths where the primary cause was due to AEs occurred on Study 202 (pneumonia 
[Patient 
], pulmonary embolism [Patient 
]). None of these two deaths were 
considered related to study drug by the investigator.

All deaths in Studies 01, 102 and 202 were reviewed to ensure no casualty with tebentafusp; 
narratives were written on all of these patients and included in the relevant CSRs. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment:

Table 43 summarizes deaths occurring in patients enrolled to Study 202 based on reporting in 
the ADSL. Of patients enrolled to the tebentafusp arm, 84 patients (34%) had died as of data 
cutoff. As noted by the Applicant, almost all patients died due to disease progression. In the 
tebentafusp arm, 2 patients (0.8%) died as a result of adverse event, only one of these occurred 
within 30 days of the last dose of tebentafusp.

Cause of Death: Progressive Disease 
 
Reviewer Assessment: Not related

72 year old gentleman. The patient’s ongoing medical 
conditions at study entry included Vitamin D deficiencies. 
Reported site of disease at study entry included liver. 
 
Patient received a total of 6  doses of tebentafusp. The 
patient discontinued due to disease progression. At 35‐36 
days after the last dose, the patient experienced Grade 3 
general health deterioration, asthenia, decreased appetite, 
Grade 4 AST increase, worsening (Grade 3) 
hyperbilirubinemia, and AST elevation.  
 
At 48 days after the last dose of study drug, the patient died 
due to disease progression.

failure contributed to the patient’s 
death.

56 year old gentleman with PMHx significant for epilepsy 
and hypertension. Reported site of disease at study entry 
included liver. 
 
Patient received a total of 3 doses of tebentafusp.  Within 3‐
4 days of the first dose of tebentafusp, the patient 
experienced Grade 1 creatinine increased, Grade 3 
hyperbilirubinemia, elevated AST and ALT. Cycle 1 Days 8 
and 15 doses were omitted and the events of 
hyperbilirubinemia and creatinine increase resolved.  Cycle 2 
Day 1 dose was reduced to 20 mcg. Cycle 2 Day 2, the 
patient experienced Grade 4 hypotension, elevated 
creatinine, elevated LDH. Cycle 2 Day 8 dose was omitted. 
Within 3 days of Cycle 2 Day 15 dose, the patient 
experienced Grade 3 hepatic failure. Imaging  around that 
time showed disease progression. 11 days after the last dose 
of study drug, the patient died due to progressive disease.

Cause of Death: Unknown 
 
Investigator Assessment: Not related. 
The Investigator assessed the cause of 
death as most likely due to disease 
progression as suggested by the

ascites, and increased edema of the 
lower extremities.  
 
Reviewer Assessment: Not related. 
Based on the AE reporting and the 
overall clinical picture, most likely 
cause of death is progressive disease.

64 year old gentleman with ongoing medical conditions at 
study entry including rheumatoid arthritis, AST increased, 
fatigue, and abdominal pain. Sites of disease at study entry 
included liver, lung, and lymph node.  
 
The patient received a total of 5 doses of tebentafusp. On 
Cycle 2 Day 12, treatment was discontinued due to patient 
decision. Prior to discontinuing treatment, the patient 
experienced Grade 3 AST and bilirubin elevation, Grade 3 
tumor lysis syndrome. At the time of treatment 
discontinuation, the following non‐serious AEs were 
ongoing: dizziness, nausea, constipation, ascites, abdominal 
distension, muscular weakness, AST increased, 
hyperbilirubinemia, peripheral edema. Approximately 3 
weeks after discontinuing treatment, the patient withdrew 
consent for further study‐related procedures and visits. No 
imaging to assess disease progression was performed. 27 
days after the last dose of study drug, the patient died. 
Cause of death was reported as unknown.

For the ISS_Pool dataset, 0.8% of patients (4/505) had fatal adverse events. These were intra‐
abdominal hemorrhage, malignant melanoma, pneumonia, and pulmonary embolism. The 
event of abdominal hemorrhage occurred in a 44 year old gentleman with a diagnosis of 
metastatic cutaneous melanoma. The patient received his first dose of tebentafusp at a dose of 
0.0006 mg/kg. Six days later, the patient experienced fatal abdominal hemorrhage after 
presenting to the emergency department with right upper quadrant pain, dyspnea, hypoxia, 
and no palpable pulse. The patient died despite aggressive resuscitation. The cause of death 
was stated as abdominal hemorrhage, possibly related to liver biopsy performed before study 
treatment was given and repeated 2 days later. Both the Investigator and Sponsor assessed the

n = number of patients; PT = preferred term; SOC = system organ class; TEAE = treatment‐emergent adverse event. 
Patients with multiple TEAEs per SOC or PT are counted only once in each row. 
Adverse events (AEs) are coded using MedDRA version 23.1. 
TEAEs are defined as any AE with a start date from day of first dose of study drug up to 90 days after last dose of 
study drug or until start of alternative cancer therapy post treatment discontinuation, whichever occurs first. 
Number (%) of patients are sorted alphabetically for SOC and by descending frequency overall for PT. A patient can 
have one or more PTs reported under a given SOC. 
Serious adverse events (SAEs) include deaths reportable as SAEs and non‐fatal SAEs. 
Source: Module 5.3.5.3, Table ISS 02.03.03.01.01. 
The Applicant’s Position:

Across all studies, 128 patients (31.2%) experienced an SAE. The most common SAEs reported 
were CRS in 29 patients (7.1%), hypotension in 11 patients (2.7%), and pyrexia in 16 patients 
(3.9%). 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
 
Table 46 shows the frequency of SAEs occurring in Study 202.  Overall, the incidence of SAEs 
was slightly higher in the tebentafusp arm compared with the IC arm. The pattern of SAEs 
between the two arms differs. The most common SAEs reported in the tebentafusp arm are 
CRS, events associated with CRS including nausea, dyspnea, and hypotension, and skin 
disorders including rash, pruritis, urticaria, and skin reaction. These SAEs were rarely reported 
in patients receiving IC.

| Table 46 shows the frequency of SAEs occurring in Study 202. Overall, the incidence of SAEs |
| was slightly higher in the tebentafusp arm compared with the IC arm. The pattern of SAEs |
| between the two arms differs. The most common SAEs reported in the tebentafusp arm are |
| CRS, events associated with CRS including nausea, dyspnea, and hypotension, and skin |
| disorders including rash, pruritis, urticaria, and skin reaction. These SAEs were rarely reported |
| in patients receiving IC. |

Source: Reviewer Table: Study 202 ADSL (Subject-Level Analysis Dataset) - 2021-04-21, ADAE (Adverse Events 
Analysis Dataset) - 2021-04-21. Variables used: USUBJID, TRT01A, SAFFL, TRTEMFL, AEDECOD, 
AETOXGRN, AEBODSYS, AESER 
1 Does not include investigator reported laboratory abnormalities. 
2 GT (Grouped Term) represents collection of related preferred terms. See Appendix 19.3. 
 
In the ISS_Pool, 36% of patients (182/505) had at least one SAE. The most commonly reported 
event by PT occurring in ≥ 2% of patients were CRS (6%), pyrexia (3.4%), and hypotension 
(2.8%). An additional SAE of note is multiple organ dysfunction syndrome (Grade 4) occurring in 
2 patients (0.4%).  
 
Multiple organ dysfunction syndrome occurred in 1 patient enrolled to the dose expansion 
phase of Study 102. Patient 
 was a 53 year old woman with a diagnosis of uveal 
melanoma who developed Grade 3 CRS and Grade 4 hypotension after her first dose of 
tebentafusp.  The patient was treated with fluid resuscitation, steroids, phenylephrine, 
vasopressor, and tocilizumab. The day after the infusion, the patient developed Grade 4 
multiple organ dysfunction syndrome including acute kidney injury, respiratory failure, and 
shock liver. The patient was intubated. The patient was ultimately extubated and transitioned 
to hemodialysis. The event of CRS was reported as resolved; the event of multiple organ 
dysfunction was ongoing at the time the patient was discharged to hospice. The patient 
subsequently died of progressive disease 27 days after the patient’s only dose of study drug.  
The SAE of multiple organ dysfunction syndrome was ongoing at the time of death.  
 
Multiple organ dysfunction syndrome occurred in 1 patient, a 78 year old gentleman, enrolled 
to study 201. Narrative was not available.

Reviewer Comment: Across the development program, the most frequently observed SAEs are 
consistent with those observed in Study 202 and associated with CRS. The potentially fatal

(b) (6)

#### Dropouts and/or Discontinuations Due to Adverse Effects

Data:

Table 47 presents drug-related TEAEs resulting in study drug discontinuation and is presented 
by SOC and PT for UM patients for the individual studies and all studies combined. The choice 
to present a summary of study drug discontinuation due to drug-related TEAEs per Investigator, 
rather than TEAE regardless of causality, is because this is how the investigators made the 
decision to continue or discontinue study drug during the study. In general, drug-related TEAEs 
are more likely to exclude TEAE resulting from disease progression or underlying morbidity.

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

185 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

n = number of patients; TEAE = treatment‐emergent adverse event. 
a Determined by the investigator to be possibly related, probably related, definitely related, or related to 
tebentafusp. 
Patients with multiple TEAEs per SOC or PT are counted only once in each row.  
Adverse events (AEs) are coded using MedDRA version 23.1. 
TEAEs are defined as any AE with a start date from day of first dose of study drug up to 90 days after last dose of 
study drug or until start of alternative cancer therapy post treatment discontinuation, whichever occurs first. 
Number (%) of patients are sorted alphabetically for SOC and by descending frequency overall for PT. A patient can 
have one or more PTs reported under a given SOC. 
Source: Module 5.3.5.3, Table ISS 02.03.04.02.01. 
 
The Applicant’s Position:

Across all studies, 15 patients (3.7%) experienced a TEAE that led to study drug discontinuation. 
The most common TEAE that led to study drug discontinuation regardless of causality was CRS 
in 2 patients (0.5%). 
 
Across all studies, TEAEs that led to study drug discontinuation and considered to be drug 
related by the Investigator were reported in 8 patients (2.0%). The vast majority of these AEs 
appear to be cytokine mediated.  No patients discontinued due to acute skin AEs. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
 
Table 48 summarizes the reasons for patient discontinuation from Study 202. As reported in the 
ADSL, 6 patients (2.4%) in the tebentafusp arm discontinued treatment due to AE. This was 
smaller but overall comparable to what was observed in the IC arm.

Source: Reviewer Table: Study 202 ADSL (Subject-Level Analysis Dataset) - 2021-04-21. Variables used: 
USUBJID, TRT01A, SAFFL, DCTREAS

Based on adverse event reporting (dataset ADAE), 8 patients (3.3%) in the tebentafusp arm and 
7 patients (6%) in the IC arm had at least one AE that led to treatment discontinuation.  The 
following TEAES resulted in permanent discontinuation in 1 patient (0.4%) in the tebentafusp 
arm in Study 202: anaphylactic reaction, brain edema, cytokine release syndrome, fatigue (GT), 
hepatotoxicity, hypotension (GT), nausea, spinal cord compression. No TEAE by PT led to 
discontinuation in the tebentafusp arm in more than 1 patient.

In the ISS_Pool, 5% of patients (27/505) had an adverse event leading to permanent 
discontinuation of tebentafusp.  This is similar to that observed in Study 202. Evaluation of the 
specific AEs leading to discontinuation did not identify a safety signal not identified in Study 
202.

Reviewer Comment: Tebentafusp rarely leads to treatment discontinuation.

a Determined by the investigator to be possibly related, probably related, definitely related, or related to  
Patients with multiple TEAEs per SOC or PT are counted only once in each row. 
Adverse events (AEs) are coded using MedDRA version 23.1. 
TEAEs are defined as any AE with a start date from day of first dose of study drug up to 90 days after last dose of study drug or until start of alternative cancer 
therapy post treatment discontinuation, whichever occurs first. 
Number (%) of patients are sorted alphabetically for SOC and by descending frequency overall for PT. A patient can have one or more PTs reported under a 
given SOC. 
Source: Module 5.3.5.3, Table ISS 02.03.04.04.01.

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

191 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

The Applicant’s Position:

Across all studies, 107 patients (26.1%) experienced TEAE that led to study drug 
discontinuation. The most common TEAEs that led to study discontinuation regardless of 
causality were pyrexia in 10 patients (2.9%), ALT increased in 10 patients (2.4%), and AST 
increased in 10 patients (2.4%). 
 
Across all studies, TEAEs that led to study drug interruption and considered to be drug-related 
by the Investigator were reported in 72 patients (17.6%). This approach enables identification of 
AEs the Investigator deemed drug-related vs. interruptions that are more related to disease 
progression or other comorbidities. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
 
FDA did not consider investigator assigned causality in its safety analysis.  
 
In Study 202, the percentage of patients who had at least one TEAE leading to treatment 
interruption is similar between the arms with 62 patients (25%) in the tebentafusp arm and 27 
patients (24%) in the IC arm having at least one AE leading to interruption. Table 50 summarizes 
the TEAEs leading to treatment interruption in at least 2 patients in the tebentafusp arm.   
 
In the ISS_Pool, 24% of patients (121/505) had at least one TEAE leading to treatment 
interruption. This is similar that observed in Study 202. The most common TEAE leading to 
discontinuation were consistent with those identified in Study 202, including CRS and AEs 
associated with CRS.

Table 50: Adverse Events Leading to Treatment Interruption in ≥ 2 patient in the Tebentafusp

Significant Adverse Events

The Applicant’s Position:

Table 51 presents SAEs regardless of causality in ≥ 1% of UM patients for individual studies and 
all studies combined. Across all studies, 128 patients (31.2%) experienced an SAE. The most 
common SAEs reported were CRS in 29 patients (7.1%), hypotension in 11 patients (2.7%), and 
pyrexia in 16 patients (3.9%).

n = number of patients; PT = preferred term; SOC = system organ class; TEAE = treatment-
emergent adverse event.  
Patients with multiple TEAEs per SOC or PT are counted only once in each row.  
Adverse events (AEs) are coded using MedDRA version 23.1.  
TEAEs are defined as any AE with a start date from day of first dose of study drug up to 90 days after last dose 
of study drug or until start of alternative cancer therapy post treatment discontinuation, whichever occurs first.  
Number (%) of patients are sorted alphabetically for SOC and by descending frequency overall for PT. A patient 
can have one or more PTs reported under a given SOC.  
Serious adverse events (SAEs) include deaths reportable as SAEs and non-fatal SAEs.  
Source: Module 5.3.5.3, Table ISS 02.03.03.01.01.

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

194 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

Grade 3‐4 TEAES 
 
FDA considered Grade 3‐4 TEAES as significant adverse events. Table 52 shows Grade 3‐4 
TEAEs, not including investigator AE reported laboratory abnormalities, occurring in at least 2% 
of patients in the tebentafusp arm in Study 202. A higher percentage of patients in the 
tebentafusp arm (54%) had at least one Grade 3‐4 TEAE compared to patients in the IC arm 
(34%). As seen in the analysis of SAEs, most Grade 3‐4 TEAEs in the tebentafusp arm were CSR, 
related to CSR, or related to skin toxicity. Overall, the Grade 3‐4 analysis did not identify any 
new safety signal with the exception of hypertension. In patients who received tebentafusp, 9% 
had the adverse event of hypertension. As noted by the Applicant in Section 8.2.5, because of 
the risk of hypotension associated with tebentafusp related CRS, Studies 102 and 202 required 
patients with pre‐existing hypertension to temporarily hold anti‐hypertensive medications prior 
to the first few doses of tebentafusp. This may explain the higher frequency of hypertension 
seen in the tebentafusp arm compared to the IC arm.

CTCAE = Common Terminology Criteria for Adverse Events; n = number of patients; TEAE = treatment‐emergent 
adverse event  
a Determined by the investigator to be possibly related, probably related, definitely related, or related to 
tebentafusp. 
TEAEs are defined as any adverse event (AE) with a start date from day of first dose of study drug up to 90 days 
after last dose of study drug or until start of alternative cancer therapy post treatment discontinuation, whichever 
occurs first. Patients with multiple events in the same category are counted only once in that category. Patients 
with events in more than 1 category are counted once in each of those categories. 
Serious adverse events (SAEs) include deaths as an outcome of SAEs and non‐fatal SAEs. 
Patient IMCGP100‐202‐
 had a Grade 3 TEAE of “pulmonary embolism” which increased to Grade 5. This 
patient is counted under “Grade 5”, “maximum CTCAE Grade 3 or 4”, and “TEAE leading to death” in this table. 
Source: Module 5.3.5.3, Table ISS 02.03.01.01.

n = number of patients; PT = preferred term, SOC = system organ class; TEAE = treatment‐emergent adverse event 
Patients with multiple TEAEs per SOC or PT are counted only once in each row.  
Adverse events (AEs) are coded using MedDRA version 23.1. 
TEAEs are defined as any AE with a start date from day of first dose of study drug up to 90 days after last dose of study drug or until start of alternative cancer 
therapy post treatment discontinuation, whichever occurs first. 
Number (%) of patients are sorted alphabetically for SOC and by descending frequency overall for PT. A patient can have one or more PTs reported under a 
given SOC. 
Source: Module 5.3.5.3, Table ISS 02.03.02.01.01.

NDA/BLA Multi‐disciplinary Review and Evaluation BLA 761228 
Kimmtrak (tebentafusp)

200 
Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews) 
 
Disclaimer: In this document, the sections labeled as “Data” and “The Applicant’s Position” are completed by the 
Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

The Applicant’s Position:

For all studies combined, a total of 410 patients (100.0%) who received tebentafusp experienced 
any TEAE. System organ classes in which TEAEs were reported most frequently (≥ 50% of 
patients in tebentafusp arm) were general disorders and administration site conditions (95.6%), 
skin and subcutaneous tissue disorders (94.6%), gastrointestinal disorders (83.7%), vascular 
disorders (57.8%), musculoskeletal and connective tissue disorders (57.3%), nervous system 
disorders (55.4%), and investigations (53.2%).

TEAEs (PTs) with the highest incidence (≥ 30% of patients in tebentafusp arm) were pyrexia 
(78.5%), pruritus (72.0%), fatigue (56.8%), nausea (57.1%), chills (54.9%), rash (51.0%), 
hypotension (41.5%), vomiting (36.1%), dry skin (35.1%), headache (32.9%), rash maculo-
papular (32.7%), and oedema peripheral (32.0%).

These TEAEs are consistent with the mechanism of action of tebentafusp and can generally be 
divided into 2 major categories, skin and cytokine-mediated events. Tebentafusp redirects T cells 
to gp100+ melanocytes in the skin which leads to acute skin toxicities (rash, pruritis, erythema, 
and edema). Tebentafusp also activates T cells leading to cytokine release which results in 
cytokine mediated AEs including CRS (defined by pyrexia, hypotension, and hypoxia) as well as 
other AEs likely resulting from elevated cytokines (e.g., fatigue, nausea, chills, vomiting and 
headache). Other less frequent TEAEs included LFT, ocular events, and cardiac events. 
 
Of the 410 patients who received tebentafusp, 408 (99.5%) reported TEAEs that were considered 
related to study drug by the investigator. A total of 195 patients (47.6%) had treatment-related 
TEAEs that were of Grade 3 or 4 severity. Serious TEAEs were reported in 128 patients (31.2%) 
and 90 (22.0%) reported serious TEAEs were considered related to study drug. Discontinuation 
of study drug due to TEAEs was uncommon and occurred in 15 patients (3.7%) with 8 (2.0%) 
patients experiencing events that were considered related to study drug. TEAEs resulting in death 
were reported in 1 patient (0.2%) in Study 202 (Patient 
; pulmonary embolism) and was 
considered not related to study drug by the investigator. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment:

In the ISS_Pool, 100% of the 505 patients had at least 1 TEAE. Analysis of the TEAEs at all levels 
of the MedDRA hierarchy and using SMQ analysis did not identify any new or more severe 
safety signal beyond those identified in Study 202. No rare but potentially severe or life‐
threatening adverse reactions associated with tebentafusp was identified that was not already 
observed in Study 202.  
Table 55 shows the most frequent TEAEs, occurring in ≥ 15 % of patients in the tebentafusp 
arm, for Study 202. While the percentage of patients experiencing at least 1 TEAE of any grade 
are similar between the tebentafusp and the IC arms, more patients in the tebentafusp arm had 
≥1 Grade 3‐4 TEAE compared to patients in the IC arm.

(b) (6)

| threatening adverse reactions associated with tebentafusp was identified that was not already |

Applicant and do not necessarily reflect the positions of the FDA.

In the ISS_Pool, 100% of the 505 patients had at least 1 TEAE. Analysis of the TEAEs at all levels 
of the MedDRA hierarchy and using SMQ analysis did not identify any new or more severe 
safety signal beyond those identified in Study 202. No rare but potentially severe or life‐
threatening adverse reactions associated with tebentafusp was identified that was not already 
observed in Study 202.

The Applicant’s Position:

Clinically relevant laboratory abnormalities include lymphocyte count decreased, phosphate 
decreased, lipase increased, amylase increased, ALT/AST increased, and bilirubin increased. 
Other blood chemistry changes that occurred in ≥ 20% patients receiving tebentafusp included 
hemoglobin decreased, creatinine increased, glucose increased, albumin decreased, calcium 
decreased, alkaline phosphatase increased, magnesium decreased, and sodium decreased. These 
laboratory findings were not considered clinically relevant and very few to none were ≥Grade 3. 
Clinically relevant liver function tests for labelling included AST increase, ALT increase, and 
bilirubin increase.

Overall, the clinical chemistry and hematology changes observed during Study 202 were 
generally mild to moderate in severity and required no treatment or dose modification. 
 
The FDA’s Assessment:

Table 57 summarizes the incidence of laboratory abnormalities worsening from baseline for 
Study 202. The most common worsening laboratory abnormalities, occurring in ≥50% of  
patients on the tebentafusp arm, were lymphocyte count decreased (91%), creatinine increased 
(87%), glucose increased (66%), AST increased (54%), ALT increased (52%), phosphate 
decreased (51%), and hemoglobin decreased (51%). Laboratory assessments worsening to 
Grade 3 in ≥ 5% of patients in the tebentafusp arm occurred only for lymphocyte count 
decreased (41%), lipase increased (13%), AST increased (12%), phosphate decreased (12%), and 
ALT increased (8%). Laboratory assessments worsening to Grade 4 in ≥ 5% of patients in the 
tebentafusp arm occurred only for lymphocyte count decreased (15%).

#### Vital Signs

The Applicant’s Position:

A decrease in SBP (from baseline to worst on-treatment/follow-up) of ≥ 35 mmHg was reported 
in 0 patients in the tebentafusp arm and 1 patient (0.9%) in the investigator’s choice arm; the 
latter had Grade 3 hypertension at baseline (SBP ≥ 160 mmHg).

Based on reported body temperature measurements, 77 patients (31.4%) in the tebentafusp arm 
and 108 patients (97.3%) in the investigator’s choice arm maintained normal body temperature, 
106 (43.3%) and 1 (0.9%) patients, respectively, experienced Grade 1 fever, 56 (22.9%) and 1 
(0.9%) patients, respectively, experienced Grade 2 fever, and 6 (2.4%) and 0 patients, 
respectively, experienced Grade 3 fever; no patient in either treatment arm had Grade 4 fever.

There were no notable changes between the tebentafusp and investigator’s choice arms for shifts 
from baseline to worst on-treatment/follow-up for weight, pulse rate, and respiratory rate.  
 
The FDA’s Assessment: 
FDA agrees. Transient changes in vital signs associated with CRS were frequently observed in 
patients receiving tebentafusp; however, analysis of vital signs did not identify changes in 
weight, pulse, or respiratory rate that persisted beyond resolution of CRS.

#### Electrocardiograms (ECGs)

Data: 
Cardiac AEs related to ECG abnormalities included tachycardia in 2 (10.5%) patients and sinus 
tachycardia in 1 (5.6%) patient; these events were Grade 1 or 2 in severity, non-serious, and did 
not result in discontinuation of study drug. 
 
The most commonly reported (> 1% of patients) cardiac AEs related to ECG abnormalities were 
tachycardia (11.8%), sinus tachycardia in (9.4%), sinus bradycardia (3.9%), atrial fibrillation 
(3.1%), and atrial flutter (2.4%), and supraventricular tachycardia (1.6%). No AEs of torsade’s 
de pointes were reported. Cardiac AEs of Grade ≥ 3 severity were reported in 7 patients (5.5%); 
events related to ECG abnormalities (each in 1 patient, 0.8%) included Grade 3 and Grade 4 
atrial fibrillation, atrial flutter, and tachycardia; all were considered treatment-related. Five 
patients experienced SAEs related to ECG abnormalities. 
 
In Study 102 and Study 202, there were no clinically meaningful trends in ECG findings and 
associated cardiac AEs. 
 
The Applicant’s Position:

| The FDA’s Assessment: |
|---|
| FDA agrees. Transient changes in vital signs associated with CRS were frequently observed in |
| patients receiving tebentafusp; however, analysis of vital signs did not identify changes in |
| weight, pulse, or respiratory rate that persisted beyond resolution of CRS. |

Version date: January 2020 (ALL NDA/ BLA reviews)

In Study 102 and Study 202, there were no clinically meaningful trends in ECG findings and 
associated cardiac AEs.
